Causality and Created Things
intermediate
6 min
The Motive Dichotomy: Temporal or Eternal
Razi enumerates the first branches of the fifth proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi argues that the Creator's motive for creation cannot be temporal, and if it is eternal and conditional on a temporal condition, circularity results.

Core Idea
A temporal motive leads to regress; an eternal conditional motive leads to circularity.

In this fifth proof, Razi examines the necessity of a motive for the Creator. He splits the possibilities into eternal or temporal, and shows that each path leads to an impossible consequence. This lesson focuses on the first two branches of his argument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial premise
Razi starts with the assumption that the world is temporal. From this, he deduces that the Creator must have a motive (da'in) for bringing it into existence at a specific time.
The motive is not God's essence but something that occasions the act.
2
Examine the two possibilities for the motive
The motive is either eternal or temporal. Follow the reasoning for each: if temporal, we ask about its own origin, leading to infinite regress. If eternal, consider whether it depends on a condition.
The temporal path is dismissed quickly; the focus shifts to the eternal case.
3
Understand the circularity argument
If the eternal motive is conditional upon a temporal condition, that condition must occur for the motive to be effective, but the motive must be complete for the condition to occur – this is circular (dawr).
Circularity is a logical impossibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الخامسة : لو كان العالم [ حادثاً ] وجب أن يكون للخالق : داع إلى إيجاده وتكوينه . فنقول : تلك الداعية ، إما أن تكون قديمة ، أو حادثة . فإن كانت حادثة ، عاد البحث في كيفية [ حدوثها ] وإن كانت قديمة، فإما أن تكون مشروطة بشرط ، أو لا تكون . فإن كانت مشروطة بشرط ، فذلك الشرط، إما أن يكون حادثاً أو لا يكون . فإن كان ذلك الشرط حادثاً ، لزم الدور . لأنه لا يتم الداعية التامة المؤثرة في الإحداث ، إلا عند حدوث ذلك الشرط ، وأن لا يحدث ذلك الشرط إلا إذا تمت تلك الداعية المؤثرة ، وذلك يوجب الدور ، وأما إن كان ذلك الشرط قديماً ، أو قلنا : إن تلك الداعية القديمة غير مشروطة بشرط : فالكلام في هذين القسمين واحد ، وذلك لأنه إما أن يقال : حصل في الأزل مانع ، أو لم يحصل . والأول باطل . لأن ذلك المانع إن كان واجباً لذاته ، امتنع زواله ، فوجب أن لا يزول ذلك الامتناع . وإن كان ممكناً لذاته ، افتقر إلى مؤثر . ويعود التقسيم فيه ، ولا ينقطع إلا عند الانتهاء إلى موجب واجب لذاته : ويلزم من امتناع زوال
The fifth proof (al-hujja al-khamisa): If the world were temporal (hadith), then the Creator must have a motive (da'in) for bringing it into existence and forming it. We say: that motive (da'iyya) is either eternal (qadima) or temporal (haditha). If it is temporal, the inquiry returns to how it occurred [i.e., its origination]. If it is eternal, then it is either conditional upon a condition (shart) or not. If it is conditional upon a condition, then that condition is either temporal or not. If that condition is temporal, then circularity (dawr) follows, because the fully effective motive that influences bringing into existence is not completed except when that condition occurs, and that condition does not occur except when that effective motive is completed, and that necessitates circularity. And if that condition is eternal, or if we say that the eternal motive is not conditional upon a condition: then the discussion in these two divisions is one. That is because it is either said: there occurred in eternity (al-azal) a preventing factor (mani'), or it did not occur. The first is false, because if that preventing factor is necessary by itself (wajib bi-dhatihi), its removal is impossible, so it must not be removed. And if it is possible by itself (mumkin bi-dhatihi), it is in need of an influencer (mu'aththir). And the division recurs in it, and it does not cease except when reaching a necessitator (mujib) that is necessary by itself. And from the impossibility of its removal...
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth proof in a series of arguments against the temporal creation of the world. Razi argues that if the world is temporal (hadith), then the Creator must have a motive (da'in) to bring it into existence at a specific time. He then examines the nature of this motive. If the motive itself is temporal, then we must ask how it came about, leading to an infinite regress. If the motive is eternal, we consider two possibilities: it is either conditional upon a condition or not. If it is conditional and the condition is temporal, then circularity (dawr) arises because the motive depends on the condition and the condition depends on the motive. If the condition is eternal or the motive is unconditional, then we ask whether there was a preventing factor (mani') in eternity that prevented creation. If there was no preventing factor, creation would have occurred eternally, contradicting the claim that the world is temporal. If there was a preventing factor, then that factor must be either necessary or possible. If necessary, it cannot cease, so creation cannot occur. If possible, it requires an influencer, leading to another infinite regress. The argument cuts off before completing the conclusion, but the intended implication is similar to previous arguments: temporal creation is impossible.

Key Terms
الحجة الخامسة (al-hujja al-khamisa)
حادث (hadith)
داع / داعية (da'in / da'iyya)
قديم (qadim)
شرط (shart)
دور (dawr)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that an eternal motive automatically explains a temporal creation without further conditions.
Correction: Razi shows that if the motive is eternal, it either depends on a condition or not; if conditional and the condition is temporal, circularity arises.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's fifth proof, what problem arises if the Creator's motive is eternal and conditional upon a temporal condition?
A) Infinite regress
B) Circularity
C) The motive becomes necessary
D) The condition becomes eternal

Key Takeaway
An eternal motive conditional on a temporal condition leads to circularity, which is logically impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you examine your own motives for worship, do you find any 'temporal conditions' that make your devotion conditional—such as expecting a certain outcome or feeling? How does Razi's logical proof expose the fragility of such conditional faith? What specific change can you make today to base your worship on an unconditional, eternal motive?
?
Consider a good deed you do regularly. Is your motive truly unconditional, or is it tied to a specific situation, reward, or recognition? What would it mean for your motive to be 'eternal and unconditional' like the Creator's motive? How can you detach your intention from worldly conditions starting this week?
?
The proof shows that conditional motives lead to logical absurdity. In your spiritual life, are there areas where you have made your obedience to Allah conditional on something else (e.g., 'I will pray more when I get a better job')? How does this lesson confront you with the need for a pure, unconditional intention? What is one step you can take right now to remove that condition?

Causality and Created Things
intermediate
7 min
Eternal Motive: Unconditional or with Eternal Condition
Razi continues the fifth proof by examining the unconditional/eternal condition branch and the preventing factor.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument that if the Creator's motive is eternal and unconditional (or with an eternal condition), then the absence or presence of a preventing factor in eternity leads to impossibility or infinite regress.

Core Idea
An unconditional eternal motive either faces a preventing factor or not—both lead to impossibility.

Continuing the fifth proof, Razi now considers the case where the motive is eternal and either unconditional or with an eternal condition. He then examines whether a preventing factor existed in eternity, and shows that if there was no preventing factor, creation would have occurred eternally; if there was a preventing factor, further analysis leads to another regress unless we reach a necessary being.

Guided Path
1
Merge the two remaining cases
Razi says that if the condition is eternal or the motive is unconditional, the analysis is the same. Focus on whether a preventing factor existed in eternity.
This shows that the eternal condition and no condition reduce to the same question.
2
Consider the preventing factor
If there was no preventing factor, creation would have happened in eternity—contradicting temporality. If there was a preventing factor, it must be analyzed.
The absence of a preventing factor leads to eternal creation, which is dismissed.
3
Analyze the preventing factor
If the preventing factor is necessary by itself, it cannot cease, so creation cannot occur. If it is contingent, it requires an influencer, leading to a regress that only ends at a necessary being.
The argument cuts off here, but the implication is that the preventing factor scenario is also impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الخامسة : لو كان العالم [ حادثاً ] وجب أن يكون للخالق : داع إلى إيجاده وتكوينه . فنقول : تلك الداعية ، إما أن تكون قديمة ، أو حادثة . فإن كانت حادثة ، عاد البحث في كيفية [ حدوثها ] وإن كانت قديمة، فإما أن تكون مشروطة بشرط ، أو لا تكون . فإن كانت مشروطة بشرط ، فذلك الشرط، إما أن يكون حادثاً أو لا يكون . فإن كان ذلك الشرط حادثاً ، لزم الدور . لأنه لا يتم الداعية التامة المؤثرة في الإحداث ، إلا عند حدوث ذلك الشرط ، وأن لا يحدث ذلك الشرط إلا إذا تمت تلك الداعية المؤثرة ، وذلك يوجب الدور ، وأما إن كان ذلك الشرط قديماً ، أو قلنا : إن تلك الداعية القديمة غير مشروطة بشرط : فالكلام في هذين القسمين واحد ، وذلك لأنه إما أن يقال : حصل في الأزل مانع ، أو لم يحصل . والأول باطل . لأن ذلك المانع إن كان واجباً لذاته ، امتنع زواله ، فوجب أن لا يزول ذلك الامتناع . وإن كان ممكناً لذاته ، افتقر إلى مؤثر . ويعود التقسيم فيه ، ولا ينقطع إلا عند الانتهاء إلى موجب واجب لذاته : ويلزم من امتناع زوال
The fifth proof (al-hujja al-khamisa): If the world were temporal (hadith), then the Creator must have a motive (da'in) for bringing it into existence and forming it. We say: that motive (da'iyya) is either eternal (qadima) or temporal (haditha). If it is temporal, the inquiry returns to how it occurred [i.e., its origination]. If it is eternal, then it is either conditional upon a condition (shart) or not. If it is conditional upon a condition, then that condition is either temporal or not. If that condition is temporal, then circularity (dawr) follows, because the fully effective motive that influences bringing into existence is not completed except when that condition occurs, and that condition does not occur except when that effective motive is completed, and that necessitates circularity. And if that condition is eternal, or if we say that the eternal motive is not conditional upon a condition: then the discussion in these two divisions is one. That is because it is either said: there occurred in eternity (al-azal) a preventing factor (mani'), or it did not occur. The first is false, because if that preventing factor is necessary by itself (wajib bi-dhatihi), its removal is impossible, so it must not be removed. And if it is possible by itself (mumkin bi-dhatihi), it is in need of an influencer (mu'aththir). And the division recurs in it, and it does not cease except when reaching a necessitator (mujib) that is necessary by itself. And from the impossibility of its removal...
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth proof in a series of arguments against the temporal creation of the world. Razi argues that if the world is temporal (hadith), then the Creator must have a motive (da'in) to bring it into existence at a specific time. He then examines the nature of this motive. If the motive itself is temporal, then we must ask how it came about, leading to an infinite regress. If the motive is eternal, we consider two possibilities: it is either conditional upon a condition or not. If it is conditional and the condition is temporal, then circularity (dawr) arises because the motive depends on the condition and the condition depends on the motive. If the condition is eternal or the motive is unconditional, then we ask whether there was a preventing factor (mani') in eternity that prevented creation. If there was no preventing factor, creation would have occurred eternally, contradicting the claim that the world is temporal. If there was a preventing factor, then that factor must be either necessary or possible. If necessary, it cannot cease, so creation cannot occur. If possible, it requires an influencer, leading to another infinite regress. The argument cuts off before completing the conclusion, but the intended implication is similar to previous arguments: temporal creation is impossible.

Key Terms
قديم (qadim)
شرط (shart)
مانع (mani')
واجب لذاته (wajib bi-dhatihi)
ممكن لذاته (mumkin bi-dhatihi)
مؤثر (mu'aththir)
موجب (mujib)
الأزل (al-azal)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that an eternal preventing factor can simply be removed by God's will without further analysis.
Correction: Razi shows that if the preventing factor is necessary, it cannot be removed; if contingent, it requires another influencer, leading to regress.

Checkpoint
What is the consequence if there was no preventing factor in eternity for an eternal motive?
A) Creation would not occur at all
B) Creation would occur in eternity
C) Circularity results
D) The motive becomes temporal

Key Takeaway
An unconditional eternal motive either leads to eternal creation (if no preventing factor) or an infinite regress (if a contingent preventing factor exists), both impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider something you have always taken for granted as 'just the way it is'—perhaps your own existence, your faith, or a habit. Can you trace its cause back to a necessary foundation, or do you accept an infinite regress of excuses? Today, identify one area where you rely on an unbroken chain of justifications and commit to grounding it in the One who is Necessary by Himself.
?
If your Creator's motive for creating you was eternal and unconditional, what does it mean that you exist now, after a delay? Does your life reflect the urgency of a purpose that has been waiting for you from eternity? Write down one action you will take today to align with that eternal purpose.
?
Razi's argument shows that a contingent preventing factor leads to an infinite regress unless we stop at a Necessary Being. Are there preventing factors in your own spiritual life—excuses, distractions, or sins—that you treat as self-sufficient? Repent by acknowledging that only God can remove them, and take one step to invite His removal today.

Causality and Created Things
intermediate
6 min
Refuting the Equality of Act and Refraining
Al-Matalib al-Aliyah refutes the first division (equality) with three reasons.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the view that God's act and refraining are equal is rationally impossible, based on three arguments.

Core Idea
Equality of act and refraining leads to absurdity: preponderance without cause, impossible simultaneity, and futile action.

In this proof, al-Matalib al-Aliyah examines the logical possibilities regarding God's creation. The first possibility—that creating and refraining are equal—is shown to be invalid through three compelling reasons. Let's see how.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on equality
Read the Arabic passage describing the first division (equality) and its three refutations. Then read the translation.
Notice the logical flow: the division is stated, then three separate reasons are given.
2
Understand the first reason
The first reason says equality implies preponderance without a preponderator. Ask yourself: why is that a problem?
This challenges the principle of sufficient reason.
3
Understand the second reason
The second reason: origination is a preponderance, but preponderance cannot occur while equality holds. This is a logical contradiction.
Think about the timing: if both sides are equal, favoring one violates equality.
4
Understand the third reason
The third reason: equal act and refraining makes the act futile and the agent foolish, which cannot apply to God.
This appeals to divine wisdom and the consensus of rational people.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السادسة : كونه تعالى محدثاً للعالم، وكونه غير محدث له. إما أن يتساويا من كل الوجوه، أو يكون الإحداث أفضل مطلقاً، أو يكون الترك أفضل مطلقاً، أو يقال : الفعل أفضل في بعض الأوقات، والترك أفضل في البعض. أما القسم الأول : وهو أن يقال : استوى الطرفان واعتدل الجانبان من غير رجحان : فهذا باطل لوجوه : الأول : إنه يقتضي رجحان أحد طرفي الممكن على الآخر، لا لمرجح. وإن جاز ذلك، فلم لا يجوز رجحان وجود العالم على عدمه لا لمرجح ؟ والثاني : وهو أن الإيجاد ترجيح الجانب الوجود، وحصول الترجيح حال حصول الإستواء : محال. والثالث : إن الذي يكون فعله وتركه، متساويين في كل الجهات، كان فعله عبثاً، وكان فاعله سفيهاً ولا يكون حكيماً، ولا يكون فعله إحساناً. وأجمع العقلاء على أنه يجب تنزيه إله العالم عن مثل هذه الصفة. وأما القسم الثاني : وهو أن يقال : الفعل أفضل من الترك. فنقول : فعلى هذا التقدير، يلزم أن يقال : إنه تعالى كان تاركاً للجانب الأفضل مدة غير متناهية، ثم انتقل من الأفضل إلى الأخس، وذلك باطل. وأما القسم الثالث : وهو أن يقال : الترك أفضل من الفعل مطلقاً فنقول : يلزم أن يقال : إن خالق العالم قد انتقل من الأشرف إلى الأخس وذلك أيضاً باطل. وأما القسم الرابع : وهو أن يقال : كان الترك أفضل في بعض الأوقات، ثم صار الفعل أفضل من الترك في سائر الأوقات، فنقول : هذا أيضاً باطل. لأن هذا التبديل، إن وقع لذاته، فليجز تبدل العدم بالوجود لذاته، وهو باطل. وإن وقع بجعل جاعل، فحينئذ يعود التقسيم الأول : وهو أن الخالق إنما خصص أولوية الترك بالأوقات السالفة، وأولوية الفعل بالأوقات الحاضرة. لأن الأولى بالأوقات السالفة، عدم تلك الأولوية. والأولى بهذه الأوقات الحاضرة، أولوية الفعل، وحينئذ يعود التقسيم الأول فيه. وذلك يوجب الذهاب إلى ما لا نهاية له. والله أعلم
The sixth proof: His being, exalted, the originator of the world, and His being not its originator. Either they are equal in every respect, or origination is absolutely better, or refraining is absolutely better, or it is said: the act is better in some times and refraining is better in others. As for the first division: that it is said: the two sides are equal and the two aspects are balanced without preponderance: this is invalid for reasons: The first: it implies the preponderance of one side of the possible over the other without a preponderator. And if that is permissible, then why is the preponderance of the existence of the world over its non-existence without a preponderator not permissible? The second: that origination is a preponderance of the side of existence, and the occurrence of preponderance at the time of equality is impossible. The third: that whose act and refraining are equal in every respect, his act is futile, and his doer is foolish, and he is not wise, and his act is not beneficence. And the rational people have agreed that it is necessary to exalt the God of the world above such an attribute. As for the second division: that it is said: the act is better than refraining. We say: on this assumption, it follows that it should be said: He, exalted, was refraining from the better side for an infinite duration, then He moved from the better to the inferior, and that is invalid. As for the third division: that it is said: refraining is absolutely better than the act. We say: it follows that it should be said: the Creator of the world has moved from the nobler to the inferior, and that is also invalid. As for the fourth division: that it is said: refraining was better at some times, then the act became better than refraining at other times. We say: this is also invalid. Because this change, if it occurred for His essence, then let the change of non-existence into existence for His essence be permissible, and it is invalid. And if it occurred by the making of a maker, then the first division returns: that the Creator only assigned the priority of refraining to past times, and the priority of the act to present times. Because what is fitting for the past times is the absence of that priority. And what is fitting for these present times is the priority of the act. Then the first division returns in it. And that leads to infinite regress. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth proof (al-hujjah al-sadisah) concerning God's creation of the world. It examines four logical divisions regarding whether God's act of origination (al-ihdath) or refraining from it (al-tark) is better. The proof is structured as a refutation of all possibilities except the necessity of creation from eternity (implicitly). The first division is that act and refraining are equal in every respect. This is refuted with three reasons: (1) it implies preponderance without a preponderator, which if allowed would permit the world's existence without a cause; (2) preponderance cannot occur while equality holds; (3) if act and refraining are equal, the act is futile (عبث) and the agent foolish (سفيه), not wise or beneficent—all rational people agree God is above this. The second division holds that the act is absolutely better. This entails that God refrained from the better for an infinite duration and then moved to the inferior—clearly impossible. The third division holds that refraining is absolutely better. This entails that the Creator moved from the nobler to the inferior—also invalid. The fourth division is that refraining was better at some times and then the act became better. If this change is for God's essence, then the change of non-existence into existence would be permissible for His essence, which is invalid. If it occurs by a maker, then the first division returns (since the maker would assign priority to different times), leading to infinite regress. Thus, all divisions are refuted, supporting the conclusion that creation must have been eternal or necessary.

Key Terms
التساوي
الرجحان
العبث
السفه
الإيجاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if God's act and refraining are equally possible, then God could choose either without reason, implying randomness.
Correction: The passage shows that equality leads to absurdities: it either denies causality, involves a logical contradiction, or attributes futility to God.

Checkpoint
According to the first reason, what does equality of act and refraining imply?
A) God's act is wise
B) Preponderance without a preponderator
C) God's refraining is better
D) Infinite regress

Key Takeaway
The first division (equality) is refuted by three arguments: it violates the principle of sufficient reason, entails a logical contradiction, and implies divine folly.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever treated a decision in your life as if it didn't matter whether you did it or not—as if both options were equal? How does this lesson force you to reconsider the weight of your choices, knowing that even God's act is not neutral?
?
When you look at the universe, do you ever catch yourself thinking it could have just as easily not existed? How does the absurdity of preponderance without a preponderator strengthen your conviction that creation is deliberate and purposeful?
?
If you are honest, are there areas of your obedience to God where you treat acting and refraining as equally acceptable? How does the argument that equality implies foolishness challenge you to take a decisive stand for what is pleasing to Him?

Refuting the Remaining Three Possibilities
Al-Matalib al-Aliyah refutes the second, third, and fourth divisions with brief but powerful arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the views that act is absolutely better, refraining is absolutely better, or that superiority changes over time are all invalid.

Core Idea
If God's act is better, why did He delay? If refraining is better, why did He create? Change leads to infinite regress.

After refuting equality, the proof turns to three other possibilities. Each leads to contradictions about God's wisdom and eternity. This lesson unpacks those arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on second, third, and fourth divisions
Read the Arabic passage from 'وأما القسم الثاني' to the end, then read the translation.
Notice the pattern: each division is stated, then immediately refuted.
2
Understand the second division refutation
If the act is absolutely better, then God's refraining for infinite time meant He chose the inferior, which is impossible for a wise being.
This highlights the problem of a delay in acting on what is better.
3
Understand the third division refutation
If refraining is absolutely better, then God's creation would be a move from the nobler to the inferior, also impossible.
This is symmetric to the second refutation.
4
Understand the fourth division refutation
If superiority changes over time, we ask: does the change occur for God's essence or by an external maker? Both lead to absurdities: the first allows change in God's essence, the second leads to infinite regress.
This is the most complex refutation; focus on the dilemma.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السادسة : كونه تعالى محدثاً للعالم، وكونه غير محدث له. إما أن يتساويا من كل الوجوه، أو يكون الإحداث أفضل مطلقاً، أو يكون الترك أفضل مطلقاً، أو يقال : الفعل أفضل في بعض الأوقات، والترك أفضل في البعض. أما القسم الأول : وهو أن يقال : استوى الطرفان واعتدل الجانبان من غير رجحان : فهذا باطل لوجوه : الأول : إنه يقتضي رجحان أحد طرفي الممكن على الآخر، لا لمرجح. وإن جاز ذلك، فلم لا يجوز رجحان وجود العالم على عدمه لا لمرجح ؟ والثاني : وهو أن الإيجاد ترجيح الجانب الوجود، وحصول الترجيح حال حصول الإستواء : محال. والثالث : إن الذي يكون فعله وتركه، متساويين في كل الجهات، كان فعله عبثاً، وكان فاعله سفيهاً ولا يكون حكيماً، ولا يكون فعله إحساناً. وأجمع العقلاء على أنه يجب تنزيه إله العالم عن مثل هذه الصفة. وأما القسم الثاني : وهو أن يقال : الفعل أفضل من الترك. فنقول : فعلى هذا التقدير، يلزم أن يقال : إنه تعالى كان تاركاً للجانب الأفضل مدة غير متناهية، ثم انتقل من الأفضل إلى الأخس، وذلك باطل. وأما القسم الثالث : وهو أن يقال : الترك أفضل من الفعل مطلقاً فنقول : يلزم أن يقال : إن خالق العالم قد انتقل من الأشرف إلى الأخس وذلك أيضاً باطل. وأما القسم الرابع : وهو أن يقال : كان الترك أفضل في بعض الأوقات، ثم صار الفعل أفضل من الترك في سائر الأوقات، فنقول : هذا أيضاً باطل. لأن هذا التبديل، إن وقع لذاته، فليجز تبدل العدم بالوجود لذاته، وهو باطل. وإن وقع بجعل جاعل، فحينئذ يعود التقسيم الأول : وهو أن الخالق إنما خصص أولوية الترك بالأوقات السالفة، وأولوية الفعل بالأوقات الحاضرة. لأن الأولى بالأوقات السالفة، عدم تلك الأولوية. والأولى بهذه الأوقات الحاضرة، أولوية الفعل، وحينئذ يعود التقسيم الأول فيه. وذلك يوجب الذهاب إلى ما لا نهاية له. والله أعلم
The sixth proof: His being, exalted, the originator of the world, and His being not its originator. Either they are equal in every respect, or origination is absolutely better, or refraining is absolutely better, or it is said: the act is better in some times and refraining is better in others. As for the first division: that it is said: the two sides are equal and the two aspects are balanced without preponderance: this is invalid for reasons: The first: it implies the preponderance of one side of the possible over the other without a preponderator. And if that is permissible, then why is the preponderance of the existence of the world over its non-existence without a preponderator not permissible? The second: that origination is a preponderance of the side of existence, and the occurrence of preponderance at the time of equality is impossible. The third: that whose act and refraining are equal in every respect, his act is futile, and his doer is foolish, and he is not wise, and his act is not beneficence. And the rational people have agreed that it is necessary to exalt the God of the world above such an attribute. As for the second division: that it is said: the act is better than refraining. We say: on this assumption, it follows that it should be said: He, exalted, was refraining from the better side for an infinite duration, then He moved from the better to the inferior, and that is invalid. As for the third division: that it is said: refraining is absolutely better than the act. We say: it follows that it should be said: the Creator of the world has moved from the nobler to the inferior, and that is also invalid. As for the fourth division: that it is said: refraining was better at some times, then the act became better than refraining at other times. We say: this is also invalid. Because this change, if it occurred for His essence, then let the change of non-existence into existence for His essence be permissible, and it is invalid. And if it occurred by the making of a maker, then the first division returns: that the Creator only assigned the priority of refraining to past times, and the priority of the act to present times. Because what is fitting for the past times is the absence of that priority. And what is fitting for these present times is the priority of the act. Then the first division returns in it. And that leads to infinite regress. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth proof (al-hujjah al-sadisah) concerning God's creation of the world. It examines four logical divisions regarding whether God's act of origination (al-ihdath) or refraining from it (al-tark) is better. The proof is structured as a refutation of all possibilities except the necessity of creation from eternity (implicitly). The first division is that act and refraining are equal in every respect. This is refuted with three reasons: (1) it implies preponderance without a preponderator, which if allowed would permit the world's existence without a cause; (2) preponderance cannot occur while equality holds; (3) if act and refraining are equal, the act is futile (عبث) and the agent foolish (سفيه), not wise or beneficent—all rational people agree God is above this. The second division holds that the act is absolutely better. This entails that God refrained from the better for an infinite duration and then moved to the inferior—clearly impossible. The third division holds that refraining is absolutely better. This entails that the Creator moved from the nobler to the inferior—also invalid. The fourth division is that refraining was better at some times and then the act became better. If this change is for God's essence, then the change of non-existence into existence would be permissible for His essence, which is invalid. If it occurs by a maker, then the first division returns (since the maker would assign priority to different times), leading to infinite regress. Thus, all divisions are refuted, supporting the conclusion that creation must have been eternal or necessary.

Key Terms
الترك
الأفضل
الأخس
الأشرف
التبديل
الذات
الجاعل
التسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the consequence of the second division (act absolutely better)?
A) God would be an oppressor
B) God delayed the better for infinite time
C) The world would not exist
D) God's essence would change

Key Takeaway
The second and third divisions are symmetric: both impute a move from better to worse. The fourth division collapses into either an impossible change in God's essence or an infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt that God delayed in answering your prayers, and thought that if only He had acted sooner, it would have been better? How does the refutation of the second division challenge that assumption about divine wisdom?
?
Do you sometimes imagine that God's will changes based on your deeds—like if you sin, He might withhold mercy, or if you repent, He might grant it? The fourth division's refutation shows that such a change would either alter God's essence or require an infinite chain of causes. How does this truth confront your tendency to think of God as reactive to your actions rather than eternally wise?
?
When you face trials, do you ever question why God created you at this particular time, with these specific difficulties? The third division's refutation reminds us that God's act of creation is the noblest, not inferior. Can you accept that your existence at this moment is the better choice, even when it feels otherwise? What would it mean for you to live with that conviction today?

The Seventh Proof: Existence Opens the Door to Afflictions
Al-Matalib al-Aliyah argues that bringing into existence entails harms unfitting for a wise agent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the seventh proof from al-Matalib al-Aliyah which argues that existence entails harms, and why a wise agent would not choose to create voluntarily.

Core Idea
Existence inevitably brings fear and pain; non-existence has none — so creation is harmful.

This lesson presents a philosophical argument that challenges the idea of voluntary creation. The proof, known as the 'Seventh Proof' in al-Matalib al-Aliyah, contends that bringing something into existence inevitably opens the door to afflictions. As you read, consider how the author uses the contrast between existence and non-existence to build his case.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof statement
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the key claim: 'Origination is opening the door to afflictions.'
2
Understand the reasoning
Follow the explanation: existence brings fear of losing benefits and fear of pain. Fear itself is pain and harm.
Notice the conditional: pain requires existence and life.
3
Compare with non-existence
The author contrasts existence with non-existence. In non-existence, there is no pain at all because pain requires existence.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The proof concludes that existence is evil (sharr). Consider why a wise agent would avoid creating evil.
This sets up a problem for voluntary creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السابعة : الإيجاد فتح لباب الآفات. وذلك لا يليق بالفاعل الحكيم. بيان الأول : أن عند حصول الوجود، يحصل الخوف من زوال مصالح الوجود، ويحصل الخوف من حصول الألم. والخوف : ألم وضرر. وأما في حال العدم، فما كان فيه مؤلم أصلا، لأن حصول الألم مشروط بحصول الوجود والحياة، فعند عدم الوجود، امتنع حصول الضرر والخوف. فيثبت أن عند حصول الوجود يحصل الضرر، وعند العدم، لا ضرر. فكان هذا الوجود شراً
The seventh proof: Origination is opening the door to afflictions. And that is not fitting for the wise agent. Explanation of the first: that upon the occurrence of existence, there occurs fear of the loss of the benefits of existence, and there occurs fear of the occurrence of pain. And fear is pain and harm. And as for the state of non-existence, there was no pain at all, because the occurrence of pain is conditional upon the occurrence of existence and life. So upon the absence of existence, the occurrence of harm and fear is impossible. So it is established that upon the occurrence of existence, harm occurs, and upon non-existence, no harm. So this existence is evil.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh proof (al-hujjah al-sabi'ah) that God's creation of the world is not fitting for a wise agent because it opens the door to afflictions (al-afat). The proof is a brief argument: when existence occurs, fear of losing the benefits of existence and fear of pain arise. Fear itself is a pain and harm. In non-existence, there is no pain at all, since pain requires existence and life. Therefore, existence necessarily involves harm, whereas non-existence has no harm. Hence, existence is evil (sharr). The implication is that a wise and benevolent agent would not bring about evil, so creation must be necessary or from eternity to avoid this problem. This argument is a standard theological objection to voluntary creation, often answered by distinguishing types of existence or by asserting that the benefits outweigh the harms. The text simply presents the argument without immediate refutation.

Key Terms
الإيجاد
الآفات
الخوف
الألم
الضرر
العدم
الوجود
الشر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument claims that God is evil.
Correction: The argument is a philosophical problem: a wise agent would not voluntarily create something that brings harm. It does not directly say God is evil, but rather that voluntary creation would be inconsistent with wisdom.

Checkpoint
What is the main claim of the seventh proof?
A) Existence is always beneficial.
B) Non-existence is free from harm.
C) God must create to show His power.
D) Fear is a benefit of existence.

Key Takeaway
The seventh proof argues that existence inevitably brings harm through fear and pain, while non-existence is free from harm, concluding that creation is evil and thus unfitting for a wise agent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If existence inevitably brings fear and pain, why do you believe a wise and merciful God chose to create you? Does your life reflect gratitude for existence or resentment of its hardships?
?
The passage says fear of loss is itself a harm. What are you most afraid of losing in your life? How does that fear shape your actions, and how might trusting in God's wisdom change that?
?
You are alive, with all the pain and fear that existence entails. What is your personal answer to the claim that existence is evil? How does your faith give meaning to the unavoidable harms of life?

First Objection: Evil as Non-Existence and Its Answer
Objection and answer on evil as non-existence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objection that evil is only non-existence does not undermine God's wisdom, and how the author distinguishes between evil in essence/attributes (non-existential) and evil in actions (existential pain).

Core Idea
Evil in actions is real pain, not mere absence—showing that existence can involve genuine evil.

The objection claims that evil is simply non-existence, so how can existence be called evil? The author responds by clarifying that rational people refer to pain as evil, which is real. Then he provides a sophisticated classification: evil in essence or attributes is indeed non-existence (like blindness), but evil in actions is existential pain. This distinction resolves the apparent contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the first objection
The objector argues that all evil is non-existence. The example of killing shows that the evil is the cessation of life, which is non-existence, not the existence of the act itself.
2
Understand the author's answer
The author first states that 'evil' in common usage means pain, which is existential. Then he divides evil into three categories: in essence, attributes, and actions.
Notice the shift from purely verbal debate to a substantive distinction.
3
Identify the three categories
Evil in essence and attributes is non-existence (like blindness). Evil in actions is causing pain, which is existential. This solves the objector's dilemma.
This classification is key to the author's argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا : السؤال عليه من وجوه :
الأول : أن الشر : عبارة عن العدم. ألا ترى أن من قتل إنساناً . فذلك القتل شر ، وليس هو شر ، لأن الآلة كانت قطاعة ، ولا لأجل أن القائل كان قادراً على استعمال تلك الآلة القطاعة بالقوة القوية ، ولا لأجل أن عنق المقتول ، كان قابلاً للقطع ، فإن كل ذلك خيرات . بل إنما كان شراً ، لأنه زالت الحياة عن المحل القابل للحياة ، وهذا الزوال : عدم فثبت : أن الشر ليس إلا العدم. فكيف يقال : الوجود شر ؟
الثاني : سلمنا أن الوجود قد يكون موصوفاً بكونه شراً ، إلا أنه وإن حصل عند الوجود أنواع الخوف والألم ، فقد حصل أيضاً أنواع السرور واللذة
فنقول :
أما الجواب عن السؤال الأول : فهو أنا نقول : مراد جميع العقلاء من الشر والمفسدة والبلاء : حصول الألم والغم . ولا شك أن الألم معنى وجودي . فيثبت : أن هذا الكلام الذي ذكرتموه في السؤال : لفظي محض . وأقول : لما كان هذا الكلام منقولاً عن أكابر الحكماء ، وجب حمله على محمل صحيح ، فنقول : الشر إما أن يقع في الذات أو في الصفات أو في الأفعال . أما الشر في الذات : فليس إلا العدم، وكذا القول في الصفات . فإن العمى والصم والبكم : شرور . وهي مفهومات عدمية ، لأن العمى عبارة عن عدم البصر ، عما من شأنه أن يكون بصيراً. وكذا القول في البواقي . وأما الشر في الأفعال : فمعناه : الإيلام. ولا شك أنه معنى موجود ، فعلى هذا يجب أن يقال : الشر في الذات والصفات . أمر عدمي . وأما في الأفعال : فلا شبهة أن الشر فيها معنى موجود
وأما السؤال الثاني : فالجواب عنه من وجوه :
الأول : أن لذات هذا العالم ، أضعف بكثير من ألم هذا العالم . والتجربة تدل عليه . فإن ألذ الأشياء : قضاء شهوة البطن ، وشهوة الفرج ونحن نعلم بالضرورة : أن ألم القولنج ، وسائر الأمراض الشديدة أصعب بكثير من لذة الأكل والوقاع .
والثاني : إن عند البقاء على العدم، لا يحصل لا اللذة ولا الألم وعند الوجود يحصل أنواع الآلام ، ولا شك أن السلامة من الألم ، أفضل من الوقوع في الألم .
والثالث : أن ما يتوهم لذة في هذا العالم ، فهو في الحقيقة عبارة عن دفع الألم. فإن لذة الأكل عبارة عن دفع ألم الجوع، لذلك فكلما كان الجوع أقوى ، كان الأكل ألذ
They said: The objection against Him has several aspects: First: That evil is an expression of non-existence. Do you not see that if someone kills a person, that killing is evil, but it is not evil because the tool was cutting, nor because the killer was able to use that cutting tool with strong force, nor because the neck of the killed was susceptible to cutting, for all of that is good. Rather, it was evil only because life left the place fit for life, and this cessation is non-existence. So it is established that evil is nothing but non-existence. So how can it be said that existence is evil? Second: We grant that existence may be described as being evil, but although when existence occurs there are types of fear and pain, there also occur types of joy and pleasure. So we say: As for the answer to the first objection: It is that we say: The meaning of all rational people from evil, corruption, and affliction is the occurrence of pain and grief. There is no doubt that pain is an existential meaning. So it is established that this statement you mentioned in the objection is purely verbal. And I say: Since this statement is transmitted from the great philosophers, it must be interpreted in a valid way. So we say: Evil is either in essence, or in attributes, or in actions. As for evil in essence, it is nothing but non-existence, and likewise in attributes. For blindness, deafness, and muteness are evils, and they are non-existential concepts, because blindness is an expression of the absence of sight in that which is fit to see. And likewise for the rest. As for evil in actions, its meaning is causing pain. There is no doubt that it is an existential meaning. So on this basis it must be said: Evil in essence and attributes is a non-existential matter. And as for actions, there is no doubt that evil therein is an existential meaning. As for the second objection, the answer has several aspects: First: That the pleasures of this world are much weaker than the pain of this world. Experience indicates this. For the most pleasurable things are satisfying the appetite of the belly and the appetite of the private part, and we know necessarily that the pain of colic and severe illnesses is much harder than the pleasure of eating and intercourse. Second: That when remaining in non-existence, neither pleasure nor pain occurs, and when existence occurs, types of pains occur, and there is no doubt that safety from pain is better than falling into pain. Third: That what is imagined to be pleasure in this world is in reality the repulsion of pain. For the pleasure of eating is the repulsion of the pain of hunger, so whenever the hunger is stronger, the eating is more pleasurable.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage presents two objections against the claim that God is wise and does not do evil, along with their answers. The first objection argues that evil is purely non-existence (ʿadam), so how can existence itself be called evil? The author responds by distinguishing between evil in essence/attributes (which is non-existence) and evil in actions (which is caused pain, an existential reality). The second objection concedes that existence may be evil but notes it also brings pleasures. The author replies with three points: 1) pleasures are weaker than pains in this world; 2) non-existence avoids both pain and pleasure, but safety from pain is preferable; 3) all pleasures are merely relief from prior pain, so they are intrinsically tied to pain. The argument follows the author's logical order: first stating the objections, then giving the answers sequentially. The structure is argumentative and explanatory, not enumerative until the three points in the second answer.

عدم

ألم
إيلام
عمى
صم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All evil is non-existence, so existence cannot be called evil.
Correction: Evil in actions is existential pain; therefore, existence can involve genuine evil.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the meaning of evil in actions?
A) Non-existence of good
B) Causing pain
C) Lack of pleasure
D) Forbidden acts

Key Takeaway
Evil in essence and attributes is non-existence, but evil in actions is real existential pain, thus the objection fails.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face personal pain—physical or emotional—do you instinctively dismiss it as 'just an absence of good'? How does acknowledging its real existence change how you turn to Allah?
?
If evil in actions is real and existential, then your own harmful actions produce real evil. Are you willing to examine your life and repent from the pain you have caused others, rather than minimizing it?
?
This lesson teaches that some evil is non-existence (like blindness) and some is existential pain. How does this distinction affect your trust in Allah's wisdom when you see suffering around you? Can you commit to seeking His wisdom rather than simply rationalizing evil away?

Second Objection: Existence Brings Pain and Its Answer
Second objection and three-part answer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three arguments the author gives to show that existence brings more pain than pleasure, and why the presence of pleasure does not justify the presence of evil.

Core Idea
Pleasures are weaker than pains, non-existence avoids both, and all pleasures are merely relief from pain.

The second objection concedes that existence may involve evil but points out that it also brings pleasures. How can existence be condemned if it includes good? The author responds with three arguments: first, pleasures are much weaker than pains; second, non-existence is free from both, and safety from pain is better; third, all pleasures are merely the removal of prior pain. Together, these show that existence is not justified by the presence of pleasures.

Guided Path
1
Read the second objection
The objector acknowledges that existence may involve evil (fear, pain) but also includes joy and pleasure. So existence cannot be entirely evil.
2
Examine the first argument
Pleasures like eating and intercourse are weak compared to severe pains like colic. Experience shows this.
The author appeals to common experience.
3
Examine the second argument
Non-existence has neither pleasure nor pain. Safety from pain is better than experiencing pain, so existence is inferior.
This is a comparative argument.
4
Examine the third argument
Pleasure is merely the removal of pain. For example, the pleasure of eating is the repulsion of hunger. So pleasure is dependent on pain.
This undermines the independent value of pleasure.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

فنقول :
أما الجواب عن السؤال الأول : فهو أنا نقول : مراد جميع العقلاء من الشر والمفسدة والبلاء : حصول الألم والغم . ولا شك أن الألم معنى وجودي . فيثبت : أن هذا الكلام الذي ذكرتموه في السؤال : لفظي محض . وأقول : لما كان هذا الكلام منقولاً عن أكابر الحكماء ، وجب حمله على محمل صحيح ، فنقول : الشر إما أن يقع في الذات أو في الصفات أو في الأفعال . أما الشر في الذات : فليس إلا العدم، وكذا القول في الصفات . فإن العمى والصم والبكم : شرور . وهي مفهومات عدمية ، لأن العمى عبارة عن عدم البصر ، عما من شأنه أن يكون بصيراً. وكذا القول في البواقي . وأما الشر في الأفعال : فمعناه : الإيلام. ولا شك أنه معنى موجود ، فعلى هذا يجب أن يقال : الشر في الذات والصفات . أمر عدمي . وأما في الأفعال : فلا شبهة أن الشر فيها معنى موجود
وأما السؤال الثاني : فالجواب عنه من وجوه :
الأول : أن لذات هذا العالم ، أضعف بكثير من ألم هذا العالم . والتجربة تدل عليه . فإن ألذ الأشياء : قضاء شهوة البطن ، وشهوة الفرج ونحن نعلم بالضرورة : أن ألم القولنج ، وسائر الأمراض الشديدة أصعب بكثير من لذة الأكل والوقاع .
والثاني : إن عند البقاء على العدم، لا يحصل لا اللذة ولا الألم وعند الوجود يحصل أنواع الآلام ، ولا شك أن السلامة من الألم ، أفضل من الوقوع في الألم .
والثالث : أن ما يتوهم لذة في هذا العالم ، فهو في الحقيقة عبارة عن دفع الألم. فإن لذة الأكل عبارة عن دفع ألم الجوع، لذلك فكلما كان الجوع أقوى ، كان الأكل ألذ
They said: The objection against Him has several aspects: First: That evil is an expression of non-existence. Do you not see that if someone kills a person, that killing is evil, but it is not evil because the tool was cutting, nor because the killer was able to use that cutting tool with strong force, nor because the neck of the killed was susceptible to cutting, for all of that is good. Rather, it was evil only because life left the place fit for life, and this cessation is non-existence. So it is established that evil is nothing but non-existence. So how can it be said that existence is evil? Second: We grant that existence may be described as being evil, but although when existence occurs there are types of fear and pain, there also occur types of joy and pleasure. So we say: As for the answer to the first objection: It is that we say: The meaning of all rational people from evil, corruption, and affliction is the occurrence of pain and grief. There is no doubt that pain is an existential meaning. So it is established that this statement you mentioned in the objection is purely verbal. And I say: Since this statement is transmitted from the great philosophers, it must be interpreted in a valid way. So we say: Evil is either in essence, or in attributes, or in actions. As for evil in essence, it is nothing but non-existence, and likewise in attributes. For blindness, deafness, and muteness are evils, and they are non-existential concepts, because blindness is an expression of the absence of sight in that which is fit to see. And likewise for the rest. As for evil in actions, its meaning is causing pain. There is no doubt that it is an existential meaning. So on this basis it must be said: Evil in essence and attributes is a non-existential matter. And as for actions, there is no doubt that evil therein is an existential meaning. As for the second objection, the answer has several aspects: First: That the pleasures of this world are much weaker than the pain of this world. Experience indicates this. For the most pleasurable things are satisfying the appetite of the belly and the appetite of the private part, and we know necessarily that the pain of colic and severe illnesses is much harder than the pleasure of eating and intercourse. Second: That when remaining in non-existence, neither pleasure nor pain occurs, and when existence occurs, types of pains occur, and there is no doubt that safety from pain is better than falling into pain. Third: That what is imagined to be pleasure in this world is in reality the repulsion of pain. For the pleasure of eating is the repulsion of the pain of hunger, so whenever the hunger is stronger, the eating is more pleasurable.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage presents two objections against the claim that God is wise and does not do evil, along with their answers. The first objection argues that evil is purely non-existence (ʿadam), so how can existence itself be called evil? The author responds by distinguishing between evil in essence/attributes (which is non-existence) and evil in actions (which is caused pain, an existential reality). The second objection concedes that existence may be evil but notes it also brings pleasures. The author replies with three points: 1) pleasures are weaker than pains in this world; 2) non-existence avoids both pain and pleasure, but safety from pain is preferable; 3) all pleasures are merely relief from prior pain, so they are intrinsically tied to pain. The argument follows the author's logical order: first stating the objections, then giving the answers sequentially. The structure is argumentative and explanatory, not enumerative until the three points in the second answer.

Key Terms
لذة
ألم
عدم
وجود
قولنج
جوع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Existence is justified because it includes pleasures that outweigh pains.
Correction: Pleasures are weaker than pains, non-existence avoids both, and pleasures are only relief from pain.

Checkpoint
According to the author's third argument, what is the reality of pleasure?
A) An independent good
B) The removal of pain
C) A divine gift
D) Non-existence

Key Takeaway
The presence of pleasure does not outweigh the pain of existence; moreover, pleasure is merely relief from pain, so existence is not vindicated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think about a pleasure you recently pursued. Was it actually a relief from some discomfort—hunger, boredom, loneliness? How does realizing that pleasure is just pain-removal change your gratitude for it?
?
If non-existence means no pain and no pleasure, and existence is filled with pain (with pleasures only temporary reliefs), why do you cling so tightly to life? What does that tell you about your trust in God's wisdom?
?
Have you ever felt that your happiest moments were still tainted by underlying unease? How does the argument that pleasures are weaker than pains resonate with your own experience? Will you let this truth make you more detached from worldly joys?

Objection on God Creating Murderers and Punishment
An objection that God creating murderers and victims leads to evil and contradicts wisdom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand a classical theological objection that challenges divine wisdom and mercy based on God creating murderers and victims.

Core Idea
God creating the murderer and victim seems to cause evil and contradict His wisdom.

This passage presents a sharp objection to the doctrine that God creates all things, including human actions. The objector argues that if God creates both the killer and the victim, and then punishes the killer, He is responsible for the evil and cannot be truly wise or merciful. This sets up a key challenge that theologians must answer.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic and translation once to grasp the overall argument. Notice the objector's chain: creation → knowledge → threat → consequence.
This objection relies on a conditional: if not created, both saved.
2
Identify the three claims
The objector makes three claims: 1) God creates the killer and victim; 2) God knows the killer will kill; 3) God threatens punishment. Then he draws a conclusion about what would have been better.
3
Reflect on the implied attributes
The objector says creation 'opens the door to evil' and that such a God would be 'harmful, not wise nor merciful.' Consider why this is a serious challenge for belief in divine justice.
Notice the objector uses the attributes 'Hakim' (Wise) and 'Rahim' (Merciful) to frame the contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال بعض من يتمسك بأمثال هذه الكلمات إنه تعالى خلق القاتل والمقتول ، والظالم والمظلوم ، وعلم أن هذا يقتل ذلك الآخر ، ثم توعد القاتل بأن قال : متى قتلت ذلك الآخر : عذبتك بالنار . فلو لم يخلقهما ، لكان قد سلم المقتول من عذاب الدنيا ، والقاتل من عذاب الآخرة . فيثبت : أن الخلق فتح لباب الشر والآفات . وذلك لا يليق بالصانع الحكيم . وأما إثبات أن إله العالم مؤذي ، وليس بحكيم ولا رحيم
And someone who adheres to such words said that He, exalted, created the killer and the killed, the oppressor and the oppressed, and knew that this one would kill that other, then threatened the killer by saying: When you kill that other, I will torture you with fire. So if He had not created them, the killed would have been saved from the punishment of this world, and the killer from the punishment of the hereafter. Thus it is established that creation opens the door to evil and afflictions. And that is not befitting the Wise Maker. And as for establishing that the God of the world is harmful, and not wise nor merciful.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage presents an objection from someone who uses such arguments (likely a critic of divine wisdom). The objector argues that God creates the killer and the victim (al-qātil wa al-maqtūl), knows the killer will kill, and then threatens the killer with punishment. The objector claims that if God had not created either, both would be saved: the victim from worldly death and the killer from eternal punishment. Hence, creation itself opens the door to evil and afflictions, which is unbecoming of a wise Maker. The objector further states that this proves the God of the world is harmful (mu'dhī) and not wise nor merciful. The passage ends without an explicit refutation in this window; it is an objection that sets up a theological problem about divine foreknowledge, creation, and justice. The author likely intends to refute it in subsequent text.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that because God creates the killer and knows the outcome, He is directly responsible for evil and therefore unjust.
Correction: The objection itself is a misconception; the passage presents it as a challenge, but theologians argue that God's wisdom and mercy operate in ways that include tests, justice, and ultimate good beyond apparent evil.

Checkpoint
According to the objection, what would have happened if God had not created the killer and victim?
A) The killer would not exist, but the victim would
B) Both would be saved from punishment
C) The killer would go to heaven
D) The victim would be resurrected

Key Takeaway
The objection claims that God creating the killer and victim leads to unnecessary evil and contradicts His wisdom and mercy, setting up a key theological problem.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If you were the killer in the objection, would you blame God for creating you and allowing the act to happen? What does that reaction reveal about your understanding of divine justice and your own responsibility?
?
The objector claims that creation itself 'opens the door to evil.' Can you still affirm that God is wise and merciful without dismissing the reality of suffering? What specific belief must you hold to answer this objection authentically?

Causality and Created Things

Second formulation: knowledge does not change the known
Razi restates the argument focusing on necessity arising from a cause other than knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi's second formulation demonstrates that the world's necessity originates from a cause other than God's knowledge, specifically His essence or attribute, leading to the conclusion of an eternal world.

Core Idea
Knowledge does not create necessity; the cause lies in God's essence or attribute.

This lesson presents Razi's second formulation of the argument that knowledge does not change the known. Building on the previous argument, Razi emphasizes that the necessity of the world's existence cannot be attributed to God's knowledge, since knowledge does not alter the quiddity of the known. Instead, that necessity must arise from another cause—either God's essence or another attribute. This leads to the conclusion that God is an essential necessitator and that the world is eternal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Focus on the logical flow.
This formulation is concise; pay attention to each premise.
2
Identify the key premises
List the premises: world is possible, knowledge doesn't change the known, necessity comes from another cause.
These premises lead to the conclusion.
3
Understand the conclusion
The conclusion is that God is an essential necessitator, which implies the perpetuity of the effect (the world).
This is a key point in the argument.
4
Reflect on implications
Consider why this argument avoids relying on knowledge and why it still leads to eternal creation.
This connects to broader theological debates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولهذا الكلام تقرير آخر ، وهو أن يقال : العالم ممكن الوجود لذاته . وقد بينا : أنه من حيث إنه معلوم الوقوع ، وجب أن يكون واجب الوقوع . والعلم لا يقلب ماهية المعلوم ولا يغيره. فهذا الوجوب ما جاء البتة من العلم ، بل جاء من سبب آخر . فهذا الوجوب يدل على أن سبباً آخر ، اقتضى صيرورة العالم ، واجب الوقوع . لأنه لما دل كونـه معلـوم الوقوع ، على كونه واجب الوقوع ، وثبت : أن المؤثر في هذا الوجود، ليس هو صفة العلم : وجب أن يكون المؤثر فيه : صفة أخرى . فيثبت : أنه تعالى موجب لوجود العالم ، إما لعين ذاته المخصوصة ، أو لصفة أخرى من صفاته. وعلى هذين التقديرين ، فالتقريب معلوم فيثبت : أنه تعالى إما أن يكون عالماً بالجزئيات ، وإما أن لا يكون . وعلى التقديرين فإنه يلزم أن يكون موجباً بالذات . ومتى كان الأمر كذلك ، لزم من دوام الموجب ، دوام المعلول
And this statement has another formulation, which is to say: the world is possible in its essence. And we have shown that inasmuch as it is known to occur, it must be necessarily occurring. And knowledge does not overturn the quiddity of the known nor change it. So this necessity has not come at all from knowledge, but rather has come from another cause. So this necessity indicates that another cause necessitated the world becoming necessarily occurring. Because when its being known to occur indicates its being necessarily occurring, and it is established that the effecter in this existence is not the attribute of knowledge, it must be that the effecter is another attribute. So it is established that He, exalted, is a necessitator of the world's existence, either by His specific essence or by another of His attributes. And on both assumptions, the reasoning is known. So it is established that He, exalted, either is knowing of particulars or not. And on both assumptions, it follows that He is an essential necessitator. And when it is so, from the perpetuity of the necessitator follows the perpetuity of the effect.
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage provides a second formulation (taqrir akhar) of the same argument. It begins by noting that the world is possible in itself (mumkin al-wujud li-dhatihi). From being known to occur, it becomes necessarily occurring. However, knowledge does not change the quiddity of the known. Therefore the necessity does not come from knowledge but from another cause. That cause must be either God's essence or another attribute. In either case, God becomes an essential necessitator (mujib bi al-dhat). Whether God knows particulars or not, the conclusion stands: if He does, the previous argument holds; if He does not, He is still a necessitator because He causes by essence. Thus the world must be eternal.

Key Terms
تقرير آخر
ممكن الوجود
معلوم الوقوع
موجب بالذات
ماهية المعلوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The necessity of the world's existence comes from God's knowledge itself.
Correction: Razi argues that knowledge does not change the known, so the necessity must originate from another cause, such as God's essence or another attribute.

Checkpoint
According to the second formulation, what is the source of the world's necessary occurrence?
A) God's knowledge
B) God's essence or another attribute
C) The world's own essence
D) Change in the known quiddity

Key Takeaway
The second formulation shows that the world's necessity arises from God's essence or attribute, not His knowledge, leading to the conclusion that the world is eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever acted as if your knowledge of a situation—your planning or foresight—is what makes it happen, forgetting that true control lies only in God's essence and attributes? How can you train your heart to rely on Him as the real Cause, not on your own understanding?
?
If God's knowledge does not change the reality of things, then what you know about yourself—your sins, your weaknesses—does not define you. But what might change is your turning to the One who has the power to transform you. What is one area of your life where you need to stop being paralyzed by what you know about yourself and instead trust in God's attribute of power (qudra) to change you?
?
The argument concludes that from God's perpetuity follows the perpetuity of creation. Yet your own existence is fleeting. Does that thought humble you or trouble you? How does the permanence of God—and His eternal act of creation—challenge your attachment to this temporary world, and what is one concrete step you can take today to shift your focus from the transient to the Eternal?

The Threefold Division Before Creation
Razi enumerates the three rational judgments for the pre-creation world and rejects two.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the initial logical division of the state of the world before its origination into necessary, impossible, and possible, and why the first two are rejected.

Core Idea
The world before creation must be possible, not necessary or impossible.

This lesson opens the first proof for the eternity of matter by considering the logical options for the world before it came into existence. The argument forces a choice: was the pre-creation world necessary, impossible, or possible? We will see why the first two options are self-contradictory, leaving possibility as the only viable starting point.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Notice the threefold division and the reasons for rejecting the first two.
The passage is concise; each word carries logical weight.
2
Identify the three possibilities
Identify the three categories: necessary, impossible, possible. Understand why necessary contradicts being originated, and why impossible cannot transition to possibility.
3
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: Why does the argument consider the state before origination? This sets the stage for the rest of the proof.
The argument assumes the world is originated as a hypothesis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الأولى: لو كان العالم حادثاً، لكان قبل حدوثه، إما أن يكون واجباً لذاته أو ممتنعاً لذاته أو ممكناً لذاته. والأول يقدح في كونه محدثاً والثاني يقتضي أنه انتقل من الامتناع الذاتي إلى الإمكان الذاتي. وإنه محال. والثالث يقتضي أنه كان قبل حدوثه ممكن الوجود لذاته. فنقول: هذا الإمكان إما أن يكون صفة راجعة إلى ذاته المخصوصة، وإما أن يقال: إنه لا معنى لهذا الإمكان إلا مجرد أن القادر قادر على إيجاده وتكوينه. والثاني باطل. لأن القادر يمكنه إيجاد الممكنات، ولا يمكنه إيجاد الممتنعات، فلولا امتياز الممكن عن الممتنع بأمر عائد إليه، وإلا لما كان كون القادر قادراً على إيجاد الممكن، أولى من كونه قادراً على إيجاد الممتنع. فيثبت أن كون الممكن ممكناً: عبارة عن صفة عائدة إليه. فنقول: هذه الصفة إما أن تكون سلبية أو ثبوتية. والأول باطل، لأن الشيء إما أن يكون ممتنع الثبوت، أو ممكن الثبوت بالإمكان العام. والامتناع عدم محض. إذ لو كان صفة ثبوتية، لكان الموصوف بتلك الصفة أولى بأن يكون ثابتاً، فيلزم أن يكون ممتنع الثبوت لذاته، واجب الثبوت لذاته، وهو محال، وإذا ثبت أنه لا واسطة بين الامتناع، وبين الإمكان العام، وثبت أن الامتناع ليس صفة ثبوتية، وجب أن يكون الإمكان العام صفة ثابتة. ضرورة أنه لا بد وأن يكون أحد النقيضين: أمراً ثابتاً. فيثبت: أن الإمكان أمر عائد إلى الممكن، مغاير لكون القادر متمكناً من إيجاده، وثبت: أنه صفة ثبوتية، وثبت: أنه سابق على وجود المحدث. فتلك الصفة لا بد لها من محل موجود، وذلك المحل الموجود هو هيولى ذلك المحدث ومادته. فإنه لا معنى للهيولى إلا الذي حصل فيه إمكان حدوث تلك الصورة، فيثبت: أن كل محدث فإنه مسبوق بهيولى، ومادة. فنقول: تلك الهيولى والمادة إما أن يكون متحيزاً وإما أن لا يكون كذلك. فإن كان متحيزاً فهو الجسم، فهيولى الجسم جسم، والهيولى قديمة. فالجسم قديم. وإن لم يكن متحيزاً، فنقول: هذا باطل من وجهين: الأول: إنه يلزم أن يكون البعد والامتداد حالا في محل، لا حصول له في الحيز والجهة أصلا. وذلك محال. والثاني: إن هذه الهيولي لو صح القول به، فإن الفلاسفة أقاموا الدليل على أنه يمتنع خلوها عن الجسمية. وحينئذ لزم من قدم الهيولى، قدم الجسم. فهذا تمام القول في تقرير هذه الشبهة. واعلم أن الكلام على هذه الحجة: أن يقال: لا نسلم أن الإمكان صفة موجودة. ويدل عليه وجوه:
The first proof: If the world were originated, then before its origination it would either be necessary in itself, or impossible in itself, or possible in itself. The first contradicts its being originated. The second implies that it moved from intrinsic impossibility to intrinsic possibility, and that is impossible. The third implies that before its origination it was possible of existence in itself. So we say: This possibility is either an attribute referring to its specific essence, or it is said that this possibility has no meaning except that the powerful agent is powerful over its bringing into existence and formation. And the second is false. Because the powerful agent can bring possible things into existence, and cannot bring impossible things into existence. For if there were no distinction of the possible from the impossible by something that reverts to it, then it would not be more likely that the powerful agent is powerful over bringing the possible into existence than that it is powerful over bringing the impossible into existence. Thus it is established that the possibility of the possible is an expression of an attribute reverting to it. So we say: This attribute is either privative or positive. And the first is false, because a thing is either impossible of existence (mumtani' al-thubut) or possible of existence (mumkin al-thubut) in the general sense of possibility. And impossibility is pure non-existence. For if it were a positive attribute, then the thing described by that attribute would be more deserving of being positive, so it would follow that the impossible of existence in itself would be necessary of existence in itself, and that is impossible. And when it is established that there is no intermediary between impossibility and general possibility, and it is established that impossibility is not a positive attribute, then it is necessary that general possibility is a positive attribute. Of necessity, one of the two opposites must be something positive. Thus it is established: that possibility is something reverting to the possible, different from the fact that the powerful agent is able to bring it about; and it is established: that it is a positive attribute; and it is established: that it is prior to the existence of the originated thing. That attribute must have an existent locus, and that existent locus is the hyle and matter of that originated thing. For hyle has no meaning except that in which the possibility of the occurrence of that form inheres. Thus it is established: that every originated thing is preceded by hyle and matter. So we say: That hyle and matter are either spatial (mutahayyiz) or not. If it is spatial, then it is body, so the hyle of body is body, and the hyle is eternal. Thus body is eternal. And if it is not spatial, we say: This is false from two aspects: First: It would entail that dimension and extension inhere in a locus that has no existence in spatial location and direction at all, and that is impossible. Second: Even if this hyle were conceded to be valid, the philosophers have established the proof that it is impossible for it to be devoid of corporeality. And then from the eternity of the hyle, the eternity of body would follow. So this completes the statement in establishing this sophism. And know that the discourse on this proof is that it is said: We do not grant that possibility is an existent attribute. And there are proofs indicated for it.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage presents a philosophical proof (shubha) for the eternity of body based on the nature of possibility (imkan). The proof is laid out as a logical deduction. First, it considers the state of the world before its origination, applying the three rational judgments: necessary, impossible, and possible. The necessary is rejected because it would contradict the world being originated; the impossible is rejected because it would require a transition from impossibility to possibility, which is impossible; leaving the possible. Then it examines the nature of this possibility. It asks: Is possibility an attribute inherent in the thing's essence, or is it merely the power of an agent? The second option is refuted: if possibility were only the agent's power, there would be no distinction between the possible and the impossible, since the agent's power is indifferent. Therefore, possibility must be an attribute of the thing itself. Next, the proof asks whether this attribute is privative or positive. It argues it must be positive. The reasoning: impossibility is pure non-existence, and if it were positive, it would entail a contradiction (the impossible would be necessary). Since there is no middle between impossibility and general possibility, and impossibility is not positive, general possibility must be positive. Thus, possibility is a positive attribute distinct from the agent's power and prior to existence. This positive attribute requires an existent substrate (mahall), which is identified as the hyle (matter) of the originated thing. Hence every originated thing is preceded by matter. Then the proof considers whether this matter is spatial (mutaḥayyiz) or not. If spatial, it is body; if matter is eternal, body is eternal. If not spatial, two arguments show it is impossible: (1) extension would inhere in a non-spatial locus, which is absurd; (2) even if conceded, the philosophers have proven that hyle cannot exist without corporeality, so eternity of hyle implies eternity of body. The author then states that this completes the exposition of the sophism. He then begins his refutation by denying that possibility is an existent attribute, stating that there are proofs (wujūh) for this, which presumably follow on later pages. Note that this entire argument is presented as a shubha (sophistical argument) to be refuted, not as the author's own position.

Key Terms
الحجة
الوجوب
الامتناع
الإمكان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The world could have been necessary or impossible before creation.
Correction: The argument shows that necessary would mean uncreated, and impossible cannot transition to existence.

Checkpoint
Why does the argument reject the possibility that the world was necessary before origination?
A) Because necessity contradicts being originated.
B) Because necessity is impossible for the world.
C) Because the world is actually possible.
D) Because origination requires prior non-existence.

Key Takeaway
Before creation, the world could only have been possible in itself, not necessary or impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the world before creation could only be possible, not necessary, then your very existence is contingent every moment. Do you live with that awareness, or do you act as if you are self-sufficient? What would change today if you truly believed that without God's sustaining will, you would simply cease to be?
?
Razi rejected the idea that the world was impossible before creation because that would require an intrinsic change. How does this challenge your own assumptions about what you think is 'impossible' in your life? Are you certain that God's power can bring about what you consider impossible, without changing His essence?
?
The proof forces you to acknowledge that only God is necessary in Himself. If you truly accept that, why do you still fear created things more than you fear your Creator? Name one fear of people or circumstances that you will consciously replace with awe of God's necessity today.
