The Objection Against Pre-Existing Matter
Razi presents the second objection to the necessity of pre-existing matter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational objections raised against the necessity of pre-existing matter for origination.

Core Idea
Objections challenge whether matter necessity is self-evident or proven.

In this lesson, we study the second objection against the claim that every originated thing requires pre-existing matter. The objector argues that the premise is not self-evident because rational people disagree, and no proof has been given. He then dismantles three possible arguments for the necessity, raising critical distinctions between non-occurrence and impossibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the passage from the beginning to the end of the objection (before 'The answer'). Notice the structure: first a challenge to the premise's necessity, then a discussion of three aspects.
The objector uses a dialectical style, presenting possible counterarguments and refuting them.
2
Identify the two prongs of the challenge
The objector first argues that the premise cannot be self-evident (ضرورية) because of disagreement, and then notes that no proof has been provided. Understand why this weakens the opponent's position.
3
Analyze the three aspects
The objector addresses three possible arguments for the necessity: (1) certainty of non-occurrence, (2) analogy with a builder, (3) God's power as a free agent. For each, note the objector's rebuttal.
The first aspect is the most important: distinguishing non-occurrence from impossibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الثاني : هب أن حدوث الشيء عن المادة السابقة : معقول . فلم قلتم : إن الأمر كذلك ؟ وتقريره : إنكم إما أن تقولوا : إن هذه المقدمة ضرورية أو نظرية . لا جائز كونها ضرورية . لأن جميع المسلمين القائلين بحدوث الأجسام : اتفقوا على أنها حادثة عن محض العدم ، ولو كان افتقار الحادث إلى المادة السابقة : معلوماً بالضرورة ، لامتنع اختلاف العقلاء فيه . وأيضاً : فلو جاز لكم ادعاء البديهة في قولكم ، لجاز الخصومكم ادعاء البديهة في قولهم . فقد ظهر بهذا الكلام : فساد ادعاء الضرورة فيه . وأما الدليل . فأنتم ما ذكرتم دليلاً في أن كل محدث، فلا بد له من مادة . فقد سقط هذا الكلام ولتتكلم الآن على الوجوه التي ذكرتموها . فنقول : . أما الوجه الأول من الوجوه الثلاثة التي ذكرتموها : فجوابه : إنه لا نزاع في أن من تشكك في أن هذا الشخص الذي نشاهده الآن ، لعله حدث الآن على هذا الوجه . فإنه يقضي عليه بالجنون . إلا أن هذا ، إنما يدل على كوننا قاطعين بأن هذا المعنى لم يقع الآن . فلم قلتم : إنه يدل على كوننا قاطعين بأنه يمتنع وقوعه ؟ وتقريره : إن الوقوع غير ، وجواز الوقوع غير ، فنحن قاطعون بعدم الوقوع فأما ادعاء كوننا قاطعين بامتناع الوقوع على هذا الوجه ، فهذا ممنوع . فما الدليل عليه ؟ وأما الوجه الثاني : فلا نسلم أن حكم العقلاء بإفتقار البناء إلى المادة والمدة مثل حكمهم بإفتقار البناء إلى الباني ، والدليل عليه : أن المتكلمين قاطعون بافتقار البناء إلى الباني ، وقاطعون بإفتقاره إلى المادة والمدة . وأما الوجه الثالث : فنقول : إنه تعالى فاعل مختار ، فله أن يفعل ما شاء ، كما شاء . فلعله خلق الأجسام ابتداء ، لا عن مادة أصلا ، ثم إنه تعالى يخلق الحيوان والنبات عن مواد سابقة عليها. وأيضاً : فلعل تخليق الحيوان والنبات على هذا الوجه أصلح للمكلفين ، فلهذا السبب اختار الله تعالى تخليق هذه الأشياء في هذا العالم على هذا الوجه والله أعلم . الجواب : أما قوله : إن حدوث الشيء لا من الشيء غير معقول ، قلنا : المراد من هذه المقدمة هو أنه لا بد من ذات سابقة على حدوث الشيء ، يحصل فيها استعداد حدوثه ، سواء كانت تلك الذوات قبل حدوث هذه الصفة : موصوفة بصفة أخرى ، أو لم تكن كذلك. وظاهر : أن هذا المعنى معقول ، وغير باطل في بديهة العقل، بل كان بديهة العقل شاهدة بصحة هذا المعنى ، فإنه لا بد من سبق وجود الحديد حتى يصير فيه استعداد قبول الصورة السيفية ولا بد من سبق وجود الذهب حتى يحصل فيه استعداد قبول الصورة الخاتمية ولا بد من سبق الطين حتى يحصل فيه استعداد قبول الصورة الكوزية . وأما قوله ثانياً : إن هذا المعنى منقوض بحدوث الأعراض ، فنقول : هذا ضعيف . لأن الاعتماد في تقرير هذه الحجة : على حكم بديهة العقل فيجب أن نتعرف عن كيفية ذلك الحكم فنقول : إنا إذا عرضنا على العقل : أنه هل حدثت هذه الدار ابتداء ، من غير وجود التراب والخشب والحجر ؟ فإنا نجد جميع العقلاء جازمين بامتناعه ، مستبعدين لوقوعه . أما إذا عرضنا على العقل : أنه هل يجوز أن تجتمع هذه الأجسام ، وتحدث فيها الهيئة المخصوصة التي للدار ؟ فإنا وجدناهم قاطعين بجواز ذلك . وإذا كان التعويل
The second question: Suppose that the origination of a thing from pre-existing matter is conceivable. Why do you say that it is the case? Its explanation: You either say that this premise is necessary or theoretical. It cannot be necessary because all Muslims who say that bodies are originated agree that they are originated from pure nothingness, and if the need of the originated for pre-existing matter were known necessarily, then disagreement among rational people about it would be impossible. Also, if it were permissible for you to claim self-evidence in your statement, it would be permissible for your opponents to claim self-evidence in their statement. So by this speech the corruption of the claim of necessity in it has become clear. As for the proof, you have not mentioned any proof that every originated thing must have matter. So this speech has fallen. Now let us speak about the aspects you mentioned. We say: As for the first of the three aspects you mentioned: its answer is that there is no dispute that whoever doubts that this person we now see perhaps came to be now in this manner, he is judged insane. But this only indicates that we are certain that this meaning did not occur now. Why did you say that it indicates we are certain that its occurrence is impossible? Its explanation: occurrence is different, and possibility of occurrence is different. So we are certain of non-occurrence. As for claiming that we are certain of the impossibility of occurrence in this manner, this is denied. So what is the proof for it? As for the second aspect: we do not concede that the judgment of rational people about the need of a building for matter and time is like their judgment about the need of a building for a builder. The proof for it is that the theologians (mutakallimun) are certain of the need of a building for a builder, and they are certain of its need for matter and time. As for the third aspect: we say that He, exalted, is a free agent, so He may do what He wills as He wills. Perhaps He created bodies initially, not from any matter at all, then He, exalted, creates animals and plants from prior matters. Also, perhaps the creation of animals and plants in this manner is better for those charged (with religious obligations), so for this reason God, exalted, chose to create these things in this world in this manner, and God knows best. The answer: As for his saying: 'the origination of a thing from not a thing is inconceivable,' we say: what is meant by this premise is that there must be a prior essence (dhat) preceding the origination of the thing, in which occurs the preparedness (isti'dad) for its origination, whether those essences before the occurrence of this attribute were described by another attribute or not. It is apparent that this meaning is conceivable and not invalid in the self-evidence of the intellect; rather the self-evidence of the intellect testifies to the correctness of this meaning. For there must be the prior existence of iron so that in it occurs preparedness to receive the form of a sword, and there must be the prior existence of gold so that in it occurs preparedness to receive the form of a ring, and there must be the prior existence of clay so that in it occurs preparedness to receive the form of a pot. As for his second saying: 'this meaning is refuted by the origination of accidents,' we say: this is weak. Because the reliance in establishing this argument is on the judgment of the self-evidence of the intellect. So we must know how that judgment proceeds. We say: when we present to the intellect: 'did this house come to be initially, without the existence of earth, wood, and stone?' then we find all rational people are certain of its impossibility, deeming its occurrence far-fetched. But when we present to the intellect: 'is it permissible for these bodies to come together and for the specific configuration of the house to occur in them?' then we find them certain of the permissibility of that. And when reliance...
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection against the claim that every originated thing must have pre-existing matter, along with the response. The objector first challenges whether the premise is self-evident (ضرورية) or theoretical (نظرية). He argues it cannot be self-evident because Muslims agree bodies originate from absolute nothingness, and rational people disagree, which would not happen if it were necessary. Also, if the opponent claimed self-evidence, the opponent's opponent could claim the same. Then he notes no proof was given. He then examines three aspects (وجوه) the opponent might raise. First aspect: if someone doubted that a person now seen could have just come into existence, they'd be deemed insane, but this only proves certainty of non-occurrence, not impossibility of occurrence. Second aspect: the objector denies that the rational judgment that a building needs matter and time is as strong as the judgment that it needs a builder; theologians affirm both but the objector sees a difference. Third aspect: God is a free agent and could create bodies directly without matter; He creates animals and plants from matter possibly because it is better for human obligation. The answer begins by clarifying the meaning of the premise: it does not require creation from nothing, but that there must be a prior substance (ذات سابقة) that has the preparedness (استعداد) to receive a new form. This is rationally self-evident, illustrated by iron needing to exist before taking the form of a sword, gold for a ring, clay for a pot. The answer then addresses the objection from accidents, saying it is weak because the argument appeals to reason's immediate judgment: reason judges it impossible for a house to arise from nothing, but allows that bodies can combine to form a house. Thus the rational basis is sound.

Key Terms
المادة السابقة
البديهة
الضرورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that if we are certain something did not happen, it must be impossible.
Correction: The objector clarifies that certainty of non-occurrence does not imply impossibility; occurrence and possibility are distinct.

Checkpoint
According to the objector, why can't the premise 'every originated thing needs pre-existing matter' be self-evident?
A) Because Muslims agree bodies come from nothing.
B) Because rational people disagree about it.
C) Because no proof has been given.
D) All of the above.

Key Takeaway
The objector denies that the necessity of pre-existing matter is self-evident and argues that no valid proof has been provided.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider that God could have created everything instantly from nothing, but instead chose to work through gradual processes and matter, does your heart rest in His wisdom, or do you secretly wish for more spectacular displays of power? How can you train yourself to see His choice of means as a sign of mercy rather than a limitation?
?
The objector distinguishes between 'certain it did not happen' and 'certain it cannot happen'. Are there areas in your life where you have confused what you have never experienced with what God cannot do? What would it mean for your faith to fully embrace that with God all things are possible, even things you have never seen?
?
If you truly believed that God is a free agent who can act without any intermediate causes, how would your dua (supplication) change? Would you ask with greater expectation, knowing that He can bring about results without any material means? What holds you back from that level of certainty?

The Answer: Razi's Defense of Pre-Existing Matter
Razi clarifies the premise of pre-existing matter and defends its rational self-evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's clarification that the necessity of pre-existing matter refers to a prior essence with preparedness, and that this is rationally self-evident.

Core Idea
Pre-existing matter means a prior substance with preparedness, which is rationally clear.

In response to the objection, Razi clarifies what the premise actually means: not creation from nothing, but the need for a prior substance that has the preparedness to receive a new form. He argues that this is not only conceivable but self-evident, using examples like iron becoming a sword. He then briefly addresses the objection from accidents, though the passage ends incomplete.

Guided Path
1
Read Razi's clarification
Read the answer from the beginning. Notice how Razi redefines the premise: it requires a prior essence (ذات سابقة) with preparedness (استعداد), not creation from nothing.
This is a key move: the objector misunderstood the premise.
2
Examine the examples of self-evidence
Razi gives examples: iron for a sword, gold for a ring, clay for a pot. These show that the intellect naturally judges the need for pre-existing matter with preparedness.
Consider why these examples are persuasive.
3
Consider the response to the accidents objection
Razi briefly responds to the objection that accidents originate without matter. He says it is weak, but the passage cuts off before completing the argument.
The argument relies on reason's immediate judgment about house construction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
The self-evidence of the intellect tells you that a sword must first be iron. In the same way, what 'iron' must exist in your character before you can become the person Allah wants you to be? What will you do today to begin preparing that raw material?

Causality and Created Things

Understanding Deferred Answers in Kalam Arguments
Author records fourth objection and defers answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author defers the answer to the fourth objection and how this reflects the systematic structure of kalam works.

Core Idea
Deferring an answer is not evasion—it's systematic pedagogy.

In this passage, the author records a classic objection against the argument for the origination of bodies: prime matter is never free from accidents, which are originated, so matter itself must be originated. However, he does not answer it here. Instead, he promises a later chapter. This teaches us a key feature of scholarly writing: structuring objections and answers in their proper place.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Identify the objection and the author's response.
Notice the phrase 'فسيأتي الجواب عنه' which signals deferral.
2
Identify the objection's logic
The objection says: prime matter always has accidents (motion, rest, combination, separation); these accidents are originated; whatever is inseparable from the originated is itself originated. So matter is originated.
This is a strong objection that challenges the argument for bodies' origination.
3
Understand the deferral
The author does not answer now but refers to a later chapter on proofs for the origination of bodies. This is a deliberate structural choice.
Classical texts often group related arguments and counter-arguments together.
4
Reflect on the methodology
Why would the author defer? Consider the pedagogical benefit: thorough treatment requires systematic arrangement. This is not evasion but careful scholarship.
Think about how this approach helps the reader build understanding step by step.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قوله رابعاً : هذه الهيولى لا تخلو عن الحركة والسكون والاجتماع والافتراق . وهذه الأعراض حادثة ، وما لا ينفك عن الحادث فهو حادث . فسيأتي الجواب عنه في باب دلائل القائلين بحدوث الاجسام .
And as for his fourth saying: 'This prime matter is never free from motion and rest, combination and separation. These accidents are originated (ḥāditha), and whatever is inseparable from the originated is itself originated.' So the answer to it will come in the chapter on the proofs of those who affirm the origination of bodies.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This short passage records the fourth objection: matter always has accidents (motion, rest, etc.), which are originated, so matter must be originated as well. The author does not answer here but defers the response to a later chapter specifically on proofs for the origination of bodies. This shows the systematic structure of the work: objections that require more extensive treatment are postponed to their proper place.

Key Terms
هيولى
أعراض
حادثة
لا ينفك عن الحادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author is evading the objection because he cannot answer it.
Correction: The author explicitly promises an answer in a later chapter, which is a common practice in classical kalam to maintain systematic organization.

Checkpoint
Where does the author say the answer to the fourth objection will be provided?
A) Immediately
B) In the next paragraph
C) In the chapter on proofs for the origination of bodies
D) In the chapter on divine attributes

Key Takeaway
Deferring answers to later chapters is a deliberate pedagogical strategy in kalam works, not a sign of weakness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When someone asks you a difficult question about your faith, do you feel pressured to give an immediate answer even if you are unsure? Would you be willing to say, 'I need to study that more' and trust that a systematic approach will lead you to the truth?
?
Are there topics in your religious understanding that you have avoided because they seem too complex or uncomfortable? How can the author's patient deferral of a troubling objection inspire you to confront those topics with trust in a structured learning process?

Refuting the Third Objection on Primordial Matter
The author refutes the third objection about hayula's lack of volume.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the objection that primordial matter (hayula) cannot give rise to bodies because it lacks volume, by distinguishing between the theory of abstract substance and the theory of indivisible parts (atoms).

Core Idea
The objection only applies if hayula is an abstract substance, not if it is atoms.

In this passage, the author wraps up a previous argument based on rational intuition and then tackles the third objection against the existence of primordial matter. The objection claims that hayula cannot be the source of bodies because it has no volume or magnitude. The author shows that this objection is only valid for a specific theory of matter, not for the atomic theory held by the ancients.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two parts of the passage
Read the passage and note that it begins with a conclusion about rational intuition, then moves to the refutation of the third objection. Identify where each part starts.
The first sentence concludes the previous argument; the rest is the refutation.
2
Understand the objection
The objection states that hayula has no volume or magnitude (size), and therefore it is impossible for a body to originate from it. Consider why this seems problematic.
The objector assumes that something without size cannot combine to form something with size.
3
Distinguish between two theories
The author distinguishes two views: (1) hayula is an abstract substance without volume or location, and (2) hayula consists of indivisible parts (atoms) that are small but have some size. Note which view the objection targets.
The objection only applies to the abstract substance view.
4
See the resolution
The author argues that if hayula is made of atoms (indivisible parts), then it is clear that small parts can join to form larger bodies, just as a palace is built from small stones. The objection is thus removed.
This aligns with rational intuition, as everyone understands that large things come from small parts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في تقرير هذه الحجة، على حكم البديهة العقلية، ثم وجدنا البديهة العقلية: حكمت بالفرق بين الصورتين، فقد زال السؤال. وأما قوله ثالثاً: و الهيولى ليس لها حجم، ولا مقدار. وما كان كذلك، امتنع حدوث الجسم عنه، فنقول: إن هذا يلزم على من يقول: إن هيولى الأجسام جوهر مجرد، ليس له حجم ولا تحيز، أما من يقول: هيولى الأجسام هي الأجزاء التي لا تتجزأ، وهي التي يسميها القدماء بالهباءات. فالسؤال زائل، لأنه تقرر في عقل كل أحد: أن هذا القصر إنما تركب عن أجسام كل واحد منها في نفسه شيء صغير، إلا أنه لما انضم البعض إلى البعض حصل الجسم الكبير.
In establishing this argument, based on the judgment of rational intuition, we found that rational intuition judged a difference between the two situations, so the question is removed. And as for his third statement: 'The primordial matter has no volume and no magnitude. And whatever is like that, it is impossible for the body to originate from it,' we say: This necessarily follows for one who says that the primordial matter of bodies is an abstract substance having no volume and no spatial location. But as for one who says that the primordial matter of bodies is the indivisible parts, which the ancients call 'atoms,' then the question is removed, because it is established in the mind of everyone that this palace is only composed of bodies each of which in itself is a small thing, but when some are joined to others, the large body results.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage contains the conclusion of a previous argument and then responds to the third objection. The author first states that the argument is based on rational intuition (al-badiha al-'aqliyya), and that intuition already distinguishes between the two cases, so the objection falls away. Then he presents the objection: the claim that primordial matter (al-hayula) has no volume or magnitude, and thus it is impossible for a body to arise from it. The author replies that this objection only applies to those who hold that the primordial matter of bodies is an abstract substance without volume or location. However, for those who hold that the primordial matter of bodies is indivisible parts (i.e., atoms), which the ancients called 'ahabayat (particles), the objection does not apply. He explains that it is evident to every intellect that a large structure like a palace is composed of many small individual bodies; when they aggregate, the large body results. Thus, the objection is invalid.

Key Terms
الهيولى
الأجزاء التي لا تتجزأ
الهباءات
البديهة العقلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection about hayula having no volume applies to all theories of matter, including the atomic theory.
Correction: The author shows the objection only applies if hayula is an abstract substance without volume; if hayula consists of indivisible parts (atoms), the objection is invalid because small parts can combine to form larger bodies.

Checkpoint
According to the author, the objection about hayula having no volume applies to which theory of matter?
A) The theory of atoms (indivisible parts)
B) The theory of abstract substance
C) The theory of prime matter
D) The theory of elements

Key Takeaway
The objection that hayula lacks volume and therefore cannot form bodies is only valid for the view that hayula is an abstract substance; if hayula is composed of indivisible parts (atoms), the objection is refuted because small parts can aggregate to form larger bodies.

This lesson shows that an objection can be neutralized by distinguishing different theories. Identify one objection you've encountered and analyze what underlying assumption it relies on.

The Fourth Objection: Deferral to its Proper Place
The author presents the fourth objection and defers its answer to a later chapter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth objection against the argument from accidents and why the author strategically defers its answer.

Core Idea
Deferring an objection is a strategic move, not a concession.

In this passage, the author presents the fourth objection to his own argument. The objection claims that primordial matter (hayula) is never free from certain accidents, and since those accidents are originated, the matter itself must be originated. Rather than answering immediately, the author promises to address it later. This deferral is a common scholarly technique to keep the argument flowing and to give each objection its proper treatment.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Identify the objection's structure: premise about accidents, conclusion about hayula.
Notice the four accidents: motion/rest, conjunction/separation.
2
Understand the deferral
The author says the answer will come later. Why would he postpone? Think about the organization of kalam works: objections are handled in their relevant sections.
This is not evasion but systematic arrangement.
3
Connect to the broader argument
Reflect on how this deferral shows the interconnectedness of kalam. The argument for the origination of bodies depends on the status of accidents.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قوله رابعاً: هذه الهيولى لا تخلو عن الحركة والسكون والاجتماع والافتراق. وهذه الأعراض حادثة، وما لا ينفك عن الحادث فهو حادث. فسيأتي الجواب عنه في باب دلائل القائلين بحدوث الاجسام.
And as for his fourth statement: 'This primordial matter is not free from motion and rest, conjunction and separation. And these accidents are originated, and whatever is inseparable from the originated is itself originated.' The answer to this will come in the chapter on the proofs of those who affirm the temporal origination of bodies.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth objection and the author's response. The objection argues that primordial matter (hayula) is always accompanied by accidents (a'rad) such as motion and rest, conjunction and separation. Since these accidents are originated (haditha), whatever is never free from originated things must itself be originated. The author does not respond here but says the answer will come later in the chapter dealing with arguments for the origination of bodies (dalawil al-qa'ilin bi-huduth al-ajsam). This is a common method in kalam works of deferring a discussion to its proper section, showing the interconnectedness of arguments.

Key Terms
الهيولى
الأعراض
الحركة والسكون
الاجتماع والافتراق
الحادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author concedes the objection because he does not answer immediately.
Correction: The author systematically defers the answer to its appropriate section; this is a common scholarly technique, not a concession.

Checkpoint
Where does the author say the answer to the fourth objection will be given?
A) In the present discussion
B) In the chapter on proofs for the origination of bodies
C) In the chapter on divine attributes
D) In the chapter on prophethood

Key Takeaway
The author defers the fourth objection, demonstrating the systematic structure of kalam where arguments and objections are placed in their relevant chapters.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a difficult objection to your faith, do you demand an immediate answer or are you willing to trust that the full response may come later as you deepen your study? What does your reaction reveal about your humility before the structure of sacred knowledge?
?
Is there an area of your life where you insist on having all the answers now, rather than submitting to the process of learning step by step? How can you practice deferring your doubts to the proper time and place, as the author does here?
?
The author systematically places each argument in its chapter. Are you systematic in seeking truth, or do you jump to conclusions based on partial evidence? Commit to one specific way you will structure your search for answers this week.

The Impossibility of Custom-Contrary Events as Intuitive
Author argues rational people unanimously judge certain events impossible, establishing a strong intuitive premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why rational people intuitively judge certain events impossible and how this establishes a strong intuitive premise in theological arguments.

Core Idea
Some impossibilities are so clear that all rational minds immediately recognize them.

Before we can discuss miracles or divine power, we must first acknowledge that the human mind naturally recognizes certain things as impossible. The author presents two vivid examples to illustrate this point. As you read, notice how he uses common sense scenarios to build a foundation for his argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the two examples carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Pay attention to the two examples: the sudden appearance of an old man without parents, and rivers appearing in a desert without a source.
The author asks you to imagine presenting these scenarios to rational people.
2
Identify the universal judgment
Notice that the author claims all rational people (العقلاء بأسرهم) affirm impossibility and consider the speaker insane or lying. This is the core of his argument.
3
Reflect on the response to theologians
The author addresses a potential objection: that theologians might allow such events. He argues that outside debate, even theologians would agree with the intuitive judgment.
This shows the premise is not undermined by theological positions.
4
Consider the strength of the premise
Conclude with the author's claim that this premise is among the strongest intuitive truths (أقوى البديهيات). Understand its foundational role.
This sets the stage for further discussions on causality and miracles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الدنيا إذا كانوا بالصفة المذكورة ، ثم عرضت عليهم : أنه هل يجوز حدوث إنسان شيخ دفعة واحدة، من غير أن كان مسبوقاً بالأبوين ، ومن غير أن كان مسبوقاً بالطفولية والشباب ؟ فإنهم يقطعون بامتناعه . وإذا عرضت عليهم قول من يقول : إن هذه الأنهار جرت في هذه المفازة من غير أن يقال : إنها سالت إليها من مواضع أخر وإنما جرت في هذه المفازة على سبيل أن هذه المياه حدثت في هذه المواضع ابتداء . فإن العقلاء بأسرهم يقطعون يكون هذا القول كذباً باطلاً ، أو بأن قائل هذا القول صار مجنوناً . وهذا يدل على أن هذه المقدمة من أقوى البديهيات وأما أن المتكلمين يجوزونه ، فليس الأمر كذلك ، لأن المتكلم لو سألته عن هذه الوقائع ، لا في وقت المناظرة ، بل في وقت سلامة عقله ، لأقر بذلك ، ولقضى على من ينكروه بالجنون والعته . فثبت : أن الأمر كما ذكرناه .
If [rational] people are as described, then they are asked: Is it permissible for a man to occur as an old man all at once, without having been preceded by parents, and without having been preceded by childhood and youth? Then they affirm its impossibility. And if they are presented with the saying of one who says: These rivers flowed in this desert without it being said that they flowed to it from other places, rather they flowed in this desert in the way that these waters occurred in these places initially, then all rational people affirm that this saying is false and invalid, or that the speaker of this saying has become insane. This indicates that this premise is among the strongest of intuitive truths. As for the claim that the theologians deem it permissible, it is not so, because if you asked the theologian about these occurrences, not in the time of debate but in a time of sound mind, he would affirm that, and would judge the denier as insane and feeble-minded. So it is established that the matter is as we have stated.
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage provides two concrete examples to demonstrate that rational people universally and immediately reject certain types of events as impossible. The first example is the possibility of a full-grown human appearing without parents or a prior stage of childhood. The second is the possibility of rivers appearing in a desert without a source, as new creations on the spot. In both cases, the author claims that all rational people would affirm impossibility and consider anyone who claims otherwise as insane or lying. This shows that the premise denying such events is one of the strongest intuitive truths (badīhiyyāt). The author then addresses a potential objection that the theologians (mutakallimūn) allow such events. He responds that this is not the case: outside the context of debate, with a sound mind, any theologian would also affirm the impossibility and denounce the denier as insane. Thus, the author concludes that his claim stands.

Key Terms
بديهيات
امتناع
عقلاء
متكلمون
مناظرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the author is dismissing the possibility of miracles entirely.
Correction: The author is not denying divine power; he is establishing that rational humans naturally judge certain events as impossible in the ordinary course of nature. This serves as a premise for further arguments about causality, not a denial of miracles.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what do rational people judge about the possibility of an old man appearing all at once without parents?
A) It is possible if God wills it.
B) They affirm its impossibility.
C) They are uncertain and must investigate.
D) They judge it to be a miracle.

Key Takeaway
Rational people universally and immediately reject events that contradict the customary course of nature, establishing a strong intuitive premise for theological argumentation.

The Free Will of God Cannot Reduce Events to Pure Coincidence
Author refutes that God's free agency explains events via preponderance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that God's free will is the sole preponderator leads to the conclusion that events are pure coincidences, which cannot account for the regularity of nature.

Core Idea
Invoking arbitrary divine choice without reason cannot explain natural regularity.

The author now engages with an opponent who argues that God's status as a free agent (fā‘il mukhtār) means He simply chooses one side over the other without any external reason. The opponent believes this explains why events happen as they do. However, the author will show that this line of thinking actually implies that everything is a matter of pure coincidence, which contradicts the observable order in the world.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice the author's immediate response to the opponent's claim.
2
Identify the key claim
The opponent says God is a preponderator (murajjih) of one side over the other. What does that imply?
The author says it implies pure coincidence.
3
Understand the critique
Why does the author think this leads to coincidence? Because if the choice is not based on any reason, the outcome is arbitrary.
Coincidental matters are either impossible or, if possible, not constant or frequent.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this affects the regularity we see in nature. Does arbitrary choice account for the consistency of seasons, for example?
This connects to the broader argument against using free will as a catch-all explanation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قوله على الوجه الثالث : إنه تعالى فاعل مختار ، فيفعل ما يشاء ويحكم ما يريد ) فنقول : أما القول بأنه مرجح لاحد الطرفين على الآخر . فهذا يقتضي أن يكون وقوع أحد الجانبين : اتفاقياً محضاً ، والأمور الاتفاقية ، إما أن تكون ممتنعة الوقوع ، أو إن كانت ممكنة الوقوع ، إلا أنها لا تكون دائمة ولا أكثرية . وقوله : « إنما كانت لأنه أصلح للعباد : ففي غاية الضعف . لأن حدوث الحيوان والنبات والمعادن، لما كان موقوفاً في مجاري العادات ، على أحوال المواد ، والفصول الأربعة، وتغير أحوال الكواكب : صار ذلك سبباً لوقوع الشك العظيم، في أن المؤثر في حدوث هذه الأشياء هو الطبائع ، لا الفاعل القادر المختار . أما لو فرضنا أنها كانت تحدث من غير هذه الوسائط ، كانت العقول حاكمة . بأنه لا تعلق للطبائع بها البتة ، بل المؤثر فيها هو الفاعل المختار . فيثبت : أن هذا الطريق أصلح للمكلفين . وأيضاً : فتعليل أفعال الله تعالى بمصالح العباد ، قد أبطلناه في الفصول السالفة . والله أعلم
As for his statement in the third aspect: 'He, exalted, is a free agent, doing what He wills and judging what He desires.' We say: As for the statement that He is a preponderator of one side over the other, this requires that the occurrence of one side is pure coincidence; and matters of coincidence are either impossible to occur, or if they are possible to occur, they are neither perpetual nor frequent. His statement: 'They only occurred because it is best for the servants' is extremely weak. Because the occurrence of animals, plants, and minerals, being dependent in customary courses on the states of materials, the four seasons, and the changes of celestial bodies, has become a cause for great doubt that the effect in the occurrence of these things is natures, not the powerful free agent. But if we assumed that they occurred without these intermediaries, the intellects would rule that there is no relation of natures to them at all, but the effect in them is the free agent. So it is established that this way is more beneficial for the obligated. Also, explaining the actions of God, exalted, by the interests of servants, we have refuted in previous chapters. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage responds to a third argument from an opponent who claims that God is a free agent (fā‘il mukhtār) and does whatever He wills, and that He only creates what is best for His servants (aṣlaḥ li’l-‘ibād). The author raises two criticisms. First, he takes the claim that God is a preponderator (murajjih) of one side over the other. This, he says, would imply that the outcome is pure coincidence (ittifāqī maḥḍ). Coincidental matters are either impossible or, if possible, are not constant or frequent. This seems to argue that invoking arbitrary choice without a reason cannot account for the regularity in nature. Second, he attacks the notion that events occur because they are beneficial for servants. He notes that in the customary course, events like the generation of animals, plants, and minerals depend on material conditions, seasons, and celestial movements. This has led to the mistaken conclusion that natures (ṭabā’i‘) are the real agents, not God. If God directly created these things without intermediaries, the intellect would clearly see that natures have no role, and that direct creation is actually more beneficial for the obligated (mukallafīn) because it removes doubt. Finally, the author reminds that he has already refuted the general principle of explaining God's acts by servants' interests in earlier chapters. Thus, the opponent's argument fails.

Key Terms
فاعل مختار
مرجح
اتفاقي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God's free will alone is a sufficient explanation for why events happen, without any underlying reason.
Correction: The author shows that if God's choice is completely arbitrary, it would make events coincidental, which cannot account for the regularity we observe in nature.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what does the opponent's claim about God being a preponderator imply?
A) That God always chooses the best option.
B) That events happen by pure coincidence.
C) That God's will is constrained by external factors.
D) That nature acts independently.

Key Takeaway
The opponent's use of divine free will as a preponderator without reason reduces events to coincidence, which cannot explain the observed regularity of the world.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience an unexpected twist in your life—a sudden loss or an unearned gain—do you dismiss it as 'coincidence'? How does this lesson force you to reconsider that label and see God's deliberate wisdom instead? Will you commit today to pause and ask what wisdom He intends, rather than calling it chance?
?
You likely believe God is all-wise, yet how often do you secretly wish His choices were more predictable or more 'fair' in your eyes? This passage shows that arbitrary divine decisions would lead to chaos, not order. Can you trust that His actual decisions—even the painful ones—are not arbitrary but perfectly wise? What one area of your life do you need to surrender to His wisdom right now?
?
The opponent in this passage thought invoking free will gave God freedom, but it actually reduced His actions to randomness. Do you ever fall into the same trap—thinking that God's freedom means He can do anything without reason, forgetting that His actions are always based on His perfect attributes? How will you remind yourself of the difference between arbitrary whim and divine wisdom starting today?

The Claim That God Acts for Servants' Benefit Is Weak
Author dismisses the benefit argument as weak and causing doubt about divine agency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author dismisses the argument that events occur because they are beneficial for servants as extremely weak, and how it leads to the mistaken idea that natures are the true agents.

Core Idea
Attributing God's actions to servants' benefit invites doubt about whether natures or God cause events.

The opponent also claims that God only creates what is best (aṣlaḥ) for human beings. The author counters that this explanation is extremely weak because it creates a serious doubt: since events are usually associated with material causes, people wrongly conclude that natures (ṭabā’i‘) are the real agents. The author argues that if God directly created without intermediaries, the intellect would clearly see His agency, and that would be more beneficial for believers.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the second part of the source, starting from 'وقوله: إنما كانت لأنه أصلح للعباد'. Notice the strong language 'في غاية الضعف'.
2
Understand the doubt
The author says that because events depend on materials, seasons, etc., it creates a great doubt about who the true agent is.
People attribute causation to natures rather than God.
3
The counterfactual
If God created directly without these intermediaries, the intellect would clearly see that natures have no role.
This direct creation would be more beneficial (أصلح) for the obligated (mukallafīn).
4
Final point
The author reminds that this whole line of reasoning about benefits has already been refuted earlier.
This is a cross-reference to prior discussions.

As for his statement in the third aspect: 'He, exalted, is a free agent, doing what He wills and judging what He desires.' We say: As for the statement that He is a preponderator of one side over the other, this requires that the occurrence of one side is pure coincidence; and matters of coincidence are either impossible to occur, or if they are possible to occur, they are neither perpetual nor frequent. His statement: 'They only occurred because it is best for the servants' is extremely weak. Because the occurrence of animals, plants, and minerals, being dependent in customary courses on the states of materials, the four seasons, and the changes of celestial bodies, has become a cause for great doubt that the effect in the occurrence of these things is natures, not the powerful free agent. But if we assumed that they occurred without these intermediaries, the intellects would rule that there is no relation of natures to them at all, but the effect in them is the free agent. So it is established that this way is more beneficial for the obligated. Also, explaining the actions of God, exalted, by the interests of servants, we have refuted in previous chapters. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage responds to a third argument from an opponent who claims that God is a free agent (fā‘il mukhtār) and does whatever He wills, and that He only creates what is best for His servants (aṣlaḥ li’l-‘ibād). The author raises two criticisms. First, he takes the claim that God is a preponderator (murajjih) of one side over the other. This, he says, would imply that the outcome is pure coincidence (ittifāqī maḥḍ). Coincidental matters are either impossible or, if possible, are not constant or frequent. This seems to argue that invoking arbitrary choice without a reason cannot account for the regularity in nature. Second, he attacks the notion that events occur because they are beneficial for servants. He notes that in the customary course, events like the generation of animals, plants, and minerals depend on material conditions, seasons, and celestial movements. This has led to the mistaken conclusion that natures (ṭabā’i‘) are the real agents, not God. If God directly created these things without intermediaries, the intellect would clearly see that natures have no role, and that direct creation is actually more beneficial for the obligated (mukallafīn) because it removes doubt. Finally, the author reminds that he has already refuted the general principle of explaining God's acts by servants' interests in earlier chapters. Thus, the opponent's argument fails.

Correction: The author shows that this explanation actually creates a doubt that leads people to attribute causality to natures rather than God, and that direct causation would be even more beneficial.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Attributing God's actions to human benefit is self-defeating because it suggests natures are the real agents; direct divine intervention would be clearer and more beneficial.

The First Proof: Causality and the First Event
First proof: every temporal event needs a temporal cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument that every temporal event must have an immediate temporal cause, and how this principle refutes the idea of uncaused events or delayed causes.

Core Idea
Every temporal event demands a temporal cause—denial is irrational.

This passage presents a foundational argument in Islamic theology for the necessity of causality. The author begins by considering a hypothetical: if the world is temporal, then events must trace back to a first event. He then examines whether that first event can exist without a temporal cause. The argument uses a powerful appeal to common human intuition—anyone who perceives a new event instinctively seeks its cause. This lesson will walk you through the logic and the evidence from innate reason.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage once, then its literal translation. Notice the structure: a conditional, then two possibilities, then an explanation.
The passage is incomplete; we only have the first of two reasons.
2
Identify the two possibilities
The author states that the first temporal event either has a temporal cause or does not. Both are said to be false. Understand why 'no cause' is impossible.
3
Analyze the first reason (innate reason)
Focus on the evidence from 'badiha al-aql' and 'fitrat al-nafs'. The author gives an example: if someone claims an effect occurred without a cause, rational people would call him a liar or insane.
This shows the universality of the causal principle.
4
Reflect on the implication
If every temporal event requires a temporal cause, then the first event cannot be uncaused. This leads to a chain that must end in a necessary being (implied in the larger argument).
The argument cuts off but the conclusion is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الأولى : قالوا : لو كان العالم حادثاً ، لكانت الحوادث منتهية إلى الحادث الأول ، وذلك الحادث الأول إما أن يكون له سبب حادث ، وإما أن لا يكون ، والقسمان باطلان . فالقول بانتهاء الحوادث إلى الحادث الأول : محال . إنما قلنا : إنه تمتنع أن لا يكون للحادث الأول سبب حادث . لوجهين : الأول : إن بديهة العقل وفطرة النفس شاهدة ، بأن كل حادث فلا بد له من سبب حادث . بدليل : أن كل من أحس بحدوث حادث ، فإنه يطلب له سبباً ، ويقول : ما الذي حدث ، حتى حدث هذا الأثر ؟ ولو قال قائل إنه حدث هذا الأثر لا سبب أصلاً ، أو لسبب كان موجوداً ، قبل ذلك بمدة طويلة . فإن كل العقلاء يكذبونه . ويقولون : إنه إما أن يكذب أو مجنوناً . ولا بد لهذا الحكم من سبب حادث ، فيثبت : أن افتقار
The first proof: They said: If the world were temporal, then the temporal events would terminate at the first temporal event. And that first temporal event either has a temporal cause or does not have one. Both possibilities are false. Thus the statement that temporal events terminate at the first temporal event is impossible. We said: It is impossible that the first temporal event has no temporal cause, for two reasons: The first: The innate reason and the natural disposition of the soul testify that every temporal event necessarily has a temporal cause. The evidence: Everyone who perceives the occurrence of a temporal event seeks a cause for it and says: 'What happened such that this effect occurred?' And if someone were to say that this effect occurred with no cause at all, or with a cause that existed long before, then all rational people would declare him a liar and say that he is either a liar or insane. And this judgment necessarily requires a temporal cause. So it is established that the dependence...
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof in a section on arguments derived from motion, change, and origination (huduth). The author (or the interlocutor) begins by stating a conditional: if the world were temporal (hadith), then the series of temporal events would have to end with a first temporal event. This first temporal event must either have a temporal cause or not. Both possibilities are then declared false. The conclusion is that the claim that events terminate at a first temporal event is impossible. The author then clarifies that what is actually impossible is that the first event lacks a temporal cause. Two reasons are given. The first reason appeals to the innate reason (badiha al-aql) and natural disposition (fitrat al-nafs), which testify that every temporal event necessarily has a temporal cause. The evidence for this is empirical: anyone who witnesses an event immediately seeks its cause, asking 'What happened that caused this effect?' If someone claimed that an effect occurred without any cause, or with a cause that existed long before, all rational people would reject that person, calling him a liar or insane. Thus, the judgment that every event requires a cause is universally recognized, and this judgment itself depends on a temporal cause. The text cuts off at 'so it is established that the dependence...', but the argument continues to establish that the first event must have a cause, leading to a regress that must be resolved by a first uncaused cause (the necessary existent). This passage demonstrates the rational necessity of causality for temporal events, a foundational principle in kalam arguments for the existence of God.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument proves the world is temporal.
Correction: The argument is conditional: 'if the world is temporal, then...' It does not itself prove temporality; it is part of a broader proof that requires establishing that the world is temporal.

Checkpoint

A) Rational people would accept it as possible.
B) Rational people would declare that person a liar or insane.
C) It would prove the effect is eternal.
D) It would be a valid scientific claim.

Key Takeaway
The first proof establishes that every temporal event must have an immediate temporal cause, a principle universally recognized by innate reason.

The passage says that denying causality makes one akin to a liar or insane. Have you ever acted as if your actions have no consequences? Where in your life do you need to accept the causal link between your choices and their outcomes? Make a specific commitment to trace one effect back to its cause this week.
?
If every event requires a cause, what does that imply about your most private thoughts and intentions? Are they events that need causes? Who or what is causing them? Are you aware of the real motives behind your actions? Ask yourself: 'What is the cause of my current spiritual state?' and refuse to settle for a superficial answer.

Every New Event Requires a New Cause
Proof that the first temporal event must have a temporal cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why every temporally originated event must have a temporally originated cause, based on intuitive reason and logical analysis of the first event.

Core Idea
Every new event requires a new cause — this is a necessary judgment of reason.

This passage lays out a foundational proof for the necessity of a temporal cause for every new event. The author argues that the first temporal event itself must have a temporal cause, otherwise we fall into absurdity. Let's examine the reasoning step by step, including an objection and its refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to get the overall flow. Notice the structure: a claim, two reasons, an objection, and a response.
2
Identify the two reasons
The author gives two distinct reasons for why the first event must have a temporal cause. Find each and understand how they work. The first appeals to intuitive reason; the second is a logical dilemma.
3
Understand the objection and answer
An opponent argues that people sometimes attribute new events to old causes (e.g., old enmity). See how the author refutes this by noting that such attribution implies new conditions or removal of obstacles.
This is a common misunderstanding addressed by the text.
4
Reflect on the overall argument
Consider how this proof establishes that every temporal event depends on a prior temporal cause, leading to a chain that must have a beginning with a cause that is not itself temporally originated (the First Cause).
This connects to broader arguments for God's existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفيها وجوه : الوجوه المستنبطة من الحركة والتغير والحدوث. الحجة الأولى : قالوا : لو كان العالم حادثاً ، لكانت الحوادث منتهية إلى الحادث الأول ، وذلك الحادث الأول إما أن يكون له سبب حادث ، وإما أن لا يكون ، والقسمان باطلان . فالقول بانتهاء الحوادث إلى الحادث الأول : محال . إنما قلنا : إنه تمتنع أن لا يكون للحادث الأول : سبب حادث . لوجهين : الأول : إن بديهة العقل وفطرة النفس شاهدة ، بأن كل حادث فلا بد له من سبب حادث . بدليل : أن كل من أحس بحدوث حادث ، فإنه يطلب له سبباً ، ويقول : ما الذي حدث ، حتى حدث هذا الأثر ؟ ولو قال قائل إنه حدث هذا الأثر لا سبب أصلاً ، أو لسبب كان موجوداً ، قبل ذلك بمدة طويلة . فإن كل العقلاء يكذبونه . ويقولون : إنه إما أن يك با أو مجنوناً . ولا بد لهذا الحكم من سبب حادث ، فيثبت : أن افتقار الحادث إلى السبب الحادث، حكم ضروري في العقول ، بديهي في النفوس فكان القول : بأن الحكم الحادث غني عن السبب الحادث : قولاً باطلاً . فإن قالوا : أليس أن العقلاء يجوزون إسناد الحكم الحادث إلى سبب كان موجوداً قبل ذلك الوقت ؟ فإنهم قد يقولون : إن هذا الذي حدث الآن ، إنما حدث عن ذلك الحقد القديم، والعداوة القديمة . فهذا يدل على أنهم يجوزون إسناد الحكم الحادث، إلى سبب كان موجوداً قبله بزمان . فنقول في الجواب عنه : إن من يقول هذا الكلام لا بد وأن يعترف بأن تلك العداوة القديمة ، كانت موجبة لهذه المضار الحادثة ، إلا أن ظهور هذه الآثار عن تلك العداوة القديمة، كانت موقوفة على شرائط ، ما حدثت إلا الآن . مثل القدرة على الإظهار ، ومثل زوال الموانع . فأما أن يقال : إن تلك العداوة القديمة ، كانت توجب هذه الأحوال ، ثم كانت الشرائط بأسرها حاصلة ، والموانع بأسرها ، كانت زائلة . ثم إن ذلك الأثر ، لم يحصل مدة طويلة ، ثم حدث الآن ، لا لتغير شرط ، ولا لزوال مانع . فذلك ممتنع في بدائه العقول . فيثبت : أنه قد تقرر في العقول الصحيحة : أن كل حكم حادث فلا بد له من سبب حادث . الثاني : أن بتقدير أن لا يكون للحادث الأول سبب حادث ، لكان ذلك ، إما لأجل أن ذلك الحادث الأول له سبب، لكنه غير حادث . أما الأول فباطل بالاتفاق . وأما الثاني : فباطل أيضاً . لأن ذلك الشيء لما كان حاصلا قبل ذلك حال ما كان هذا الأثر معدوماً ، وحصل الآن أيضاً مقارناً لوجوده ، حكم صريح العقل بأنه لا يصلح لأن يكون سبباً له ، وأنه لا بد لهذا الحادث من سبب حادث.
And in it are aspects: the aspects derived from motion, change, and temporal origination. The first proof: They said: If the world were temporally originated, then temporal events would terminate at the first temporal event. That first temporal event either has a temporal cause or does not have one. Both divisions are false. So the claim that temporal events terminate at the first temporal event is impossible. We say: It is impossible that the first temporal event has no temporal cause, for two reasons: First: Indeed, the direct intuition of the intellect and the innate disposition of the soul testify that every temporal event must have a temporal cause. The evidence is that everyone who perceives the occurrence of a temporal event seeks a cause for it and says, 'What happened such that this effect occurred?' If someone were to say that this effect occurred with no cause at all, or with a cause that existed a long time before, then all rational people would consider him a liar and say that he is either a fool or insane. So this judgment necessarily requires a temporal cause. Thus it is established that the dependence of a temporal event on a temporal cause is a necessary judgment in minds and intuitive in souls. Therefore, the statement that a temporal judgment is independent of a temporal cause is false. If they say: Do rational people not permit attributing a temporal judgment to a cause that existed before that time? For they may say that what happened now occurred because of that old resentment and old enmity. This indicates that they permit attributing a temporal judgment to a cause that existed before it by a period of time. We say in response: Indeed, whoever says this must acknowledge that that old enmity was productive of these temporal harms, except that the appearance of these effects from that old enmity was dependent on conditions that only occurred now, such as the ability to manifest and the removal of obstacles. As for saying that that old enmity would produce these states while all the conditions were entirely present and all obstacles were entirely removed, yet that effect did not occur for a long time and then occurred now, not due to a change in condition nor to the removal of an obstacle, that is impossible according to the direct intuitions of minds. Thus it is established that it is settled in sound minds that every temporal judgment must have a temporal cause. The second reason: Assuming that the first temporal event has no temporal cause, that would be either because that first event has a cause, but it is non-temporal, or because it has no cause at all. The first is false by consensus. The second is also false, because since that thing existed before the state in which this effect was non-existent, and now it also occurs contemporaneous with its existence, the explicit judgment of the intellect is that it is not suitable to be a cause for it, and that this temporal event must have a temporal cause.
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof (al-ḥujja al-ūlā) from a larger discussion, likely on the impossibility of an infinite regress of causes. The proof begins by stating that if the world is temporally originated (ḥādith), then temporal events must terminate at a first temporal event (al-ḥādith al-awwal). The author then argues that this first temporal event must have a temporal cause; otherwise, it would be either uncaused or caused by a non-temporal cause. Both options are false. The main argument is supported by two reasons (wajhān). The first reason appeals to intuitive reason (badīhat al-ʿaql) and innate disposition (fiṭrat al-nafs): everyone instinctively looks for a cause when something new occurs. If someone claimed an effect had no cause or a cause from long ago, rational people would reject it. The author then anticipates an objection: people sometimes attribute a current event to an old resentment. The answer clarifies that such attribution implies the old resentment only produced the effect once suitable conditions (shurūṭ) arose and obstacles (mawāniʿ) were removed. If the old resentment had been fully capable and conditions were always present, but the effect delayed without change, that would be absurd. Thus, the necessity of a temporal cause for every temporal event is reaffirmed. The second reason is a dilemma: if the first event had no temporal cause, either it had a non-temporal cause or no cause at all. The latter is impossible by consensus. The former fails because if a non-temporal cause existed before the effect and the effect was absent, then the cause could not have produced the effect without a change, as the mind judges that an unchanging cause cannot produce a new effect. Therefore, a temporal cause is necessary.

Key Terms

السبب الحادث
بديهة العقل
فطرة النفس
شرائط
موانع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that attributing an event to an old cause (e.g., old enmity) disproves the need for a temporal cause.
Correction: Such attribution implicitly assumes new conditions or removal of obstacles, which are themselves temporal changes, thus preserving the need for a temporal cause.

C) Because the first event must be non-temporal.
D) Because rational people only accept causes that are themselves eternal.

Key Takeaway
Every temporally originated event necessarily depends on a temporally originated cause, a principle established by both intuitive reason and logical analysis.

The Objection of Old Causes and New Effects
Author refutes objection by requiring new conditions for old causes to produce new effects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the objection that new effects can arise from old causes, and why the principle 'every new event requires a new cause' remains intact.

Core Idea
An old cause cannot produce a new effect without new conditions.

This lesson presents a common objection to the principle that every new event has a new cause. Critics point to everyday examples where we attribute a present event to an old grudge. The author carefully qualifies that even in such cases, the old cause only becomes effective when new conditions arise, thereby preserving the principle.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once. Notice the structure: first the objection (if they say...), then the response (we say...).
The objection uses the example of old enmity causing new harm.
2
Identify key components
Identify the old cause (old grudge), the new effect (new harm), and the new conditions (ability to manifest, removal of obstacles). See how the author explains that the effect depends on new conditions.
The author distinguishes between a genuine case (with new conditions) and an impossible claim (without any change).
3
Understand why the objection fails
Reflect on why attributing a new effect to an old cause does not refute the principle. The old cause alone is insufficient; the new conditions are the actual new cause.
This reinforces the dictum: every new event must have a new cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا : أليس أن العقلاء يجوزون إسناد الحكم [ الحادث ] إلى سبب كان موجوداً قبل ذلك الوقت ؟ فإنهم قد يقولون : إن هذا الذي حدث الآن ، إنما حدث عن ذلك الحقد القديم، والعداوة القديمة . فهذا يدل على أنهم يجوزون إسناد الحكم الحادث، إلى سبب كان موجوداً قبله بزمان . فنقول في الجواب عنه : إن من يقول هذا الكلام [ لا بد وأن ] يعترف بأن تلك العداوة القديمة ، كانت موجبة لهذه المضار الحادثة ، إلا أن ظهور هذه الآثار عن تلك العداوة القديمة، كانت موقوفة على شرائط ، ما حدثت إلا الآن . مثل القدرة على الإظهار ، ومثل زوال الموانع . فأما أن يقال : إن تلك العداوة القديمة ، كانت توجب هذه الأحوال ، ثم كانت الشرائط بأسرها حاصلة ، والموانع بأسرها ، كانت زائلة . ثم إن ذلك الأثر ، لم يحصل مدة طويلة ، ثم حدث الآن ، لا لتغير شرط ، ولا لزوال مانع . فذلك ممتنع في بدائه العقول . فيثبت : أنه قد تقرر في العقول الصحيحة : أن كل حكم حادث فلا بد له من سبب حادث
If they say: 'Do not rational people allow attributing a new [judgment/event] to a cause that existed before that time? For they may say: This which has just occurred happened only because of that old grudge and ancient enmity. This indicates that they allow attributing the new event to a cause that existed before it by some time.' We say in response: He who says this must admit that that old enmity was necessitating these new harms, except that the appearance of these effects from that old enmity was dependent upon conditions that only occurred now, such as ability to manifest and removal of obstacles. As for the claim that that old enmity was necessitating these states, and then all conditions were present and all obstacles were removed, yet that effect did not occur for a long time and then occurred now without a change in condition or removal of an obstacle—that is impossible according to the primary intuitions of minds. Thus it is established that it is settled in sound minds that every new event must have a new cause.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer. The objection is that rational people commonly attribute a new effect (like an action) to an old cause (like a longstanding grudge). The author qualifies that even in such cases, the old cause only produces the effect when new conditions arise (e.g., opportunity, removal of obstacles). If someone claims the old cause permanently necessitates the effect without any new conditions, then the effect would have occurred earlier. Therefore, the actual cause includes the new conditions, so the dictum 'every new event requires a new cause' holds.

Key Terms
سبب حادث
حكم حادث
شرائط
موانع
بدائه العقول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection that new effects can be attributed to old causes disproves the principle that every new event has a new cause.
Correction: The author shows that even in such cases, the old cause only produces the effect when new conditions arise; thus the effective cause is actually new (the conditions).

Checkpoint
According to the answer, why does attributing a new effect to an old grudge not refute the principle that new events require a new cause?
A) Because the grudge itself is not a real cause.
B) Because the effect depends on new conditions that only arise later.
C) Because rational people are mistaken.
D) Because the grudge is not an efficient cause.

Key Takeaway
Every new event requires a new cause; apparent attribution to old causes always involves new conditions that are the real cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time you held a grudge for years and then acted on it. What new condition (opportunity, emotion, or situation) finally triggered that action? Are you willing to admit that the grudge alone was not the cause?
?
If every new effect in your life requires a new cause, what new spiritual cause have you introduced today to bring about a positive change? Or have you been waiting for an old habit to produce a new result without changing the conditions?
?
When you sin, do you blame an old weakness or circumstance, ignoring the new choice you made in that moment? Will you now trace each sin back to a new cause—your own fresh decision—and repent accordingly?

Why the First Event Needs a New Cause
The author argues that the first event must have a new cause and refutes infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the first event cannot have a permanent cause and why a new cause leads to an impossible infinite regress.

Core Idea
A permanent cause cannot explain a new event without a new cause.

In this passage, the author tackles a key question: what kind of cause can produce the very first event? He argues that a permanent cause cannot do it, because if it were sufficient, the effect would have appeared earlier. This leads to the need for a new cause, but that new cause itself must be caused, raising the problem of infinite regress. Let's follow his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two possibilities
The author first lists two possibilities for the first event: either it has no cause at all (false by consensus) or it has a cause that is not new.
The focus is on the second possibility.
2
Understand the refutation of a permanent cause
The author gives an example: Zayd's fever and the sky. The sky is permanent but did not cause the fever earlier, so it cannot be the cause now. This shows that a new effect requires a new cause.
This is a fundamental principle in Islamic theology.
3
Follow the infinite regress argument
The author then considers the possibility that the first event has a new cause. But that new cause must itself be caused, leading to an infinite chain. He raises two objections: (1) the cause would precede the first event, contradicting its 'firstness'; (2) the infinite chain either exists all at once (absurd) or as a sequence (still problematic, but the argument is cut off).
The passage ends before fully refuting the sequence option.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Second: On the assumption that the first event does not have a new cause, that would be either because that first event has no cause at all, or because it has a cause but it is not new. The first is false by consensus. The second is also false, because when that thing was present before, when this effect was nonexistent, and it now also occurs concurrent with its existence, the clear judgment of reason is that it is not fit to be a cause for it, and that this event must have another cause. Example: Zayd becomes feverish today. If we say the cause of his fever is the sky being above us and the earth below us, this is known to be invalid by intuition, because the sky being above and earth below existed for many years before this fever occurred and had no effect in causing it until that time, so it is rejected by the intuition of reason. Thus it is established that attributing a new effect to a permanent agent is known to be impossible by intuition. As for the second division: that the first event depends on a new cause—this too is invalid in several ways. First: on this assumption, its cause would precede it, so the first event would not be first; this is a contradiction. Second: on this assumption, every event must be attributed to another event before it, so these causes without end: either they exist all at once, or each later one depends on a previous one ad infinitum. The first division is invalid in several ways. First: the claim that there exist causes and effects without end all at once is invalid, as established earlier in this book. Second: on the assumption that the totality of those things occurs at that time, the question returns regarding the occurrence of that totality at that particular time...
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage presents the second main argument. It first considers the possibility that the first event has no cause (false by consensus) or has a non-new cause. The non-new cause argument is refuted by an example: a permanent cause (like the sky) cannot produce a new effect (like fever) because it existed before without producing it. Therefore, a new cause is necessary. Then the author considers the possibility that the first event has a new cause, but that leads to an infinite regress. Two objections are raised against infinite regress: (1) If the cause precedes the first event, the event is not truly first. (2) The infinite chain either exists simultaneously (absurd, as previously shown) or as a chain where each depends on a previous without end. The refutation of simultaneous existence is briefly given, and the argument for the chain is cut off at the page end. The overall conclusion is that infinite regress is impossible, but the passage is incomplete.

Misconception: An eternal cause can directly produce a new effect without any additional cause.
Correction: If an eternal cause were sufficient, it would have produced the effect earlier; thus a new condition or cause is necessary.

The first event cannot be caused by a permanent cause; a new cause is necessary, but that new cause leads to infinite regress, which is also impossible — though the full refutation is not provided here.

The Intuitive Impossibility of an Eternal Cause Producing a Temporal Effect
Example proving eternal cause cannot produce temporal effect by intuition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why an eternal cause cannot, by itself, produce a temporal effect, as demonstrated by an intuitive example.

Core Idea
An eternal cause cannot produce a temporal effect because if it could, it would have done so already.

In this lesson, we examine a concise proof from Imam al-Sanusi (or the author) that uses a simple everyday example to demonstrate an important theological principle: a permanent, unchanging cause cannot be the sole cause of a new, temporal event. The argument relies on immediate intuitive reasoning rather than complex syllogisms.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the literal meaning. Notice the example of Zayd's fever.
The passage is very short but contains a complete logical argument.
2
Identify the example and the proposed cause
The example: Zayd becomes feverish on a particular day. The proposed eternal cause: the sky being above and the earth below – a permanent fact existing for years.
The claim is that this permanent fact caused the fever.
3
Understand why the claim is intuitively false
The permanent fact existed long before the fever without producing it. If it were the cause, it would have caused the fever earlier. Intuition rejects the attribution.
This reductio argument shows the impossibility by contradiction with observed reality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سبب آخر . ومثاله : أن زيداً صار محموماً في هذا اليوم . فإن قلنا : إن سبب حدوث شاه هو كون السماء فوقنا ، والأرض تحتنا : فهذا معلوم الفساد بالبديهة . لأنه لما كان كون السماء فوقنا ، والأرض تحتنا : حاصل قبل حدوث هذه الحمى بسنين كثيرة ، ولم يكن له أثر في حدوث هذه الحمى ، إلى ذلك الوقت يكون مدفوعاً في بديهة العقل . فيثبت : أن إسناد الأثر الحادث إلى المؤثر الدائم : معلوم الامتناع بالبديهة .
Another cause. Its example: Zayd became feverish on this day. If we say: The cause of the occurrence of fever is that the sky is above us and the earth below us, this is known to be invalid by intuition. Because since the sky being above us and the earth below us existed many years before the occurrence of this fever, and it had no effect in arising this fever, until that time it is repudiated in the intuition of the intellect. So it is established: that attributing the temporal effect to the permanent cause is known to be impossible by intuition.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage presents a brief proof from example to demonstrate the impossibility of a permanent cause producing a temporal effect. The author uses a concrete example: a person (Zayd) becomes feverish on a particular day. If one were to claim that the cause of this fever is the permanent fact that the sky is above and the earth below—which has existed for many years—then this claim is immediately known to be false by intuitive reason. The reason is that this permanent condition existed long before the fever and had no effect on it. Intuition rejects that a permanent cause can produce a new temporal effect because if it could, it would have done so earlier. The conclusion is that attributing a temporal effect to a permanent cause is known by intuition to be impossible. The argument follows the structure of a reductio: the assumption leads to a contradiction with observed facts.

Key Terms
أثر حادث (temporal effect)
مؤثر دائم (permanent cause)
بديهة (intuition)
امتناع (impossibility)
فساد (invalidity)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that this argument denies all causality, meaning that nothing causes anything.
Correction: The argument only denies that an eternal, unchanging cause can alone produce a temporal effect without a temporal condition. It does not deny causality altogether; it refines our understanding of causal relationships.

Checkpoint

D) Because Zayd is not a real person.

Key Takeaway
Attributing a temporal effect to a permanent cause is impossible by intuition, as shown by the example of Zayd's fever and the unchanging sky and earth.
