First Objection Against Causal Regress: The First Event
Refutes the claim that the first temporal event requires a temporal cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the first temporal event cannot have a temporal cause, and how this refutes a specific type of causal regress.

Core Idea
The first event cannot have a temporal cause, or it wouldn't be first.

This lesson examines a simple but powerful objection to the idea that the first temporal event requires a temporal cause. The argument hinges on the definition of 'first.' If the first event had a cause, that cause would have to occur before it, contradicting its status as first. This is a classic kalam move to stop an infinite regress of causes. Let's read the passage and see how the argument is framed.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the concise style and the logical structure: a claim, then a refutation using a conditional.
The passage uses 'خلف' (contradiction) to signal the absurdity.
2
Understand the two divisions context
The passage starts with 'وأما القسم الثاني' - the second division. This refers to a previous division of types of causality. The first division presumably dealt with something else. The second division is the claim that the first temporal event needs a temporal cause.
Even without the first division, the argument is self-contained.
3
Analyze the logical structure
The argument: if the first event requires a temporal cause, then that cause would be prior to it. But then the first event would not be first. This is a contradiction. So the assumption is false.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.
4
Reflect on the broader implication
Why is this important? It blocks an infinite regress of temporal causes. The first event must be uncaused or have a non-temporal cause. This is a key move in kalam proofs for a beginning of the universe.
This connects to arguments about the eternity of the world.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القسم الثاني : وهو أن الحادث الأول يفتقر إلى سبب حادث . فهذا أيضاً باطل من وجوه : الأول : إن على هذا التقدير يكون سببه متقدماً عليه ، فلا يكون الحادث الأول حادثاً أولاً . وهذا خلف .
As for the second division: that the first temporal event requires a temporal cause. This is also invalid from [several] aspects: The first: On this assumption, its cause would be prior to it, so the first temporal event would not be the first event. This is a contradiction.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage addresses a specific claim: that the very first temporal event (the first thing to occur) needs a temporal cause itself. The author argues that this is false. If the first event had a temporal cause, that cause would have to occur before it, which would make the original event not truly the first. This is a direct contradiction (خلف). So the claim that the first event is caused by a temporal cause leads to an absurdity: the event designated as first would have a prior event, so it could not be first. The argument is simple and relies on the definition of 'first'. This is a standard kalam objection against infinite regress of causes.

Key Terms
قسم ثان (second division)
حادث أول (first temporal event)
سبب حادث (temporal cause)
متقدم (prior)
خلف (contradiction)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument denies all causality.
Correction: It only denies that the first temporal event can have a temporal cause; it does not deny causation in general.

Checkpoint
What is the contradiction (خلف) in claiming that the first temporal event requires a temporal cause?
A) A temporal cause cannot exist before the first event.
B) The cause would be prior, so the first event would not be first.
C) The first event is uncaused by definition.
D) Temporal causes can only occur after the first event.

Key Takeaway
The first temporal event cannot have a temporal cause because that would contradict its being first.

Refuting the Possibility of an Infinite Simultaneous Series
Sanusi refutes the simultaneous existence of an infinite series of temporal causes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why an actual infinite series of simultaneous temporal causes is impossible according to Sanusi's three proofs.

Core Idea
An infinite regress cannot exist all at once—it leads to logical absurdities.

The second objection argues that if every event needs a prior temporal cause, we get an infinite chain. But can that chain exist all at once? Sanusi argues no, using three proofs. This lesson unpacks those proofs and shows why only sequential dependency without a first term is possible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation once to follow the argument's flow. Notice the two possibilities presented first.
The author states the two possibilities: simultaneous or sequential.
2
Identify the two divisions
The infinite series of causes is either found all at once or sequentially. The author rejects the first.
3
Examine the three proofs against simultaneity
Three proofs are given. The first references earlier content; the second shows circularity; the third uses empirical evidence.
The second proof is particularly important: the cause would be both inside and outside the totality.
4
Grasp the conclusion
Since simultaneous existence is impossible, the series must be sequential without a first term. That's the desired result.
This sets up the objection in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : إن على هذا التقدير يجب إسناد كل حادث إلى حادث آخر قبله فهذه الأسباب التي لا نهاية لها ، إما أن توجد دفعة واحدة ، أو يكون كل متأخر مستنداً إلى ما كان متقدماً عليه ، لا إلى نهاية . والقسم الأول باطل لوجوه : الأول : إن القول بوجود أسباب ومسببات لا نهاية لها دفعة واحدة : قول باطل على ما سبق في هذا الكتاب تقريره . والثاني : إن على التقدير بكون مجموع تلك الأشياء ، إنما حدث في ذلك الوقت ، فيعود السؤال في أن حدوث ذلك المجموع في ذلك الوقت المعين ، لا بد له من سبب حادث ، وذلك باطل ، لأن ذلك السبب ، لكونه سبباً للمجموع الحوادث ، يجب أن يكون مغايراً لذلك المجموع. ولكونه حادثاً يكون داخلا في مجموع الحوادث ، فيكون الشيء الواحد ، داخلا في ذلك المجموع ، وخارجاً عنه . وهو محال . والثالث : إن الحس يدل على أن أجزاء الحركة ما حصلت على الإجتماع دفعة واحدة ، والأشخاص التي دخلت في الوجود بأسرها لم توجد مجتمعة في هذا الزمان المعين. ولما ثبت : أن كل حادث ، فلا بد من إستناده إلى حادث آخر ، لا إلى نهاية . وثبت : أن تلك الأسباب والمسببات التي لا نهاية لها ، لم توجد دفعة : وجب أن يقال : إن كل واحد منها استند إلى ما قبله لا إلى أول . وذلك هو المطلوب . فإن قالوا : فعلى هذا التقدير ، يلزم أن يكون المعدوم علة للموجود . فنقول : ليس الأمر كذلك ، بل العلة المؤثرة في وجود المعلول : هو الموجود القديم الدائم، وكل واحد من هذه الحوادث فإنه شرط لكون ذلك القديم مؤثراً في وجود الحادث المتأخر ، على ما سبق تقريره .
The second: On this assumption, every temporal event must be attributed to another temporal event before it. So these causes that have no end are either found all at once, or each later one depends on what was before it without end. The first division is false for reasons: The first: The statement that there exist causes and effects without end all at once is false, as previously established in this book. The second: On the assumption that the totality of those things occurred at that time, the question returns: the occurrence of that totality at that specific time must have a temporal cause, and this is false, because that cause, being a cause for the totality of events, must be distinct from that totality. And being temporal, it would be included in that totality of events, so a single thing would be both inside and outside that totality, which is impossible. The third: Sensory evidence indicates that parts of motion do not occur simultaneously, and all individuals that came into existence did not exist together at that specified time. And since it is established that every temporal event must rely on another temporal event without end, and it is established that those causes and effects without end did not exist at once, it must be said that each one of them relies on what is before it without a first. That is the required conclusion. If they say: On this assumption, it would follow that non-being is a cause for being. We say: It is not so. Rather, the efficient cause in the existence of the effect is the Eternal Existing One, and each one of these temporal events is a condition for that Eternal One to be efficient in the existence of the later temporal event, as previously established.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage is the second objection against the claim that the first temporal event requires a temporal cause. If that were true, it would entail an infinite regress of causes. The author analyzes whether this infinite series exists simultaneously or sequentially. He first refutes simultaneous existence with three arguments: (1) the falsity of a actually infinite set of simultaneous temporal entities, as shown earlier; (2) the totality of such events, if they occurred at once, would itself need a temporal cause, leading to a circularity where the cause is both part of and outside the totality; (3) sensory evidence shows that parts of motion and individuals do not come into existence all at once. Then he argues that since simultaneous infinite is impossible, the only option is sequential dependency without a first term. This is the required conclusion. An objection is raised: this seems to make a non-existent thing a cause. The author answers by distinguishing between the true cause (the Eternal) and temporal events being mere conditions (شروط) for God's bringing about later events. This preserves the soundness of the argument.

Key Terms
أسباب لا نهاية لها
دفعة واحدة
متأخر
محال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that an infinite regress of causes could exist all at once as a logical possibility.
Correction: The passage shows that such a simultaneous infinite leads to logical contradictions and is empirically unsupported.

Checkpoint
According to the second proof, what makes the simultaneous existence of an infinite series of temporal causes impossible?
A) The totality would be too large to exist at once.
B) The cause of the totality would be both inside and outside it.
C) Sensory evidence contradicts simultaneity.
D) The series would have no beginning.

Key Takeaway
An infinite series of temporal causes cannot exist simultaneously; it must be sequential without a first term.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your daily actions are a chain of causes and effects. Can you trace any one action back indefinitely? What does the impossibility of a simultaneous infinite series mean for the source of your own existence and choices? Will you continue to live as if your actions are self-causing?
?
The argument shows that a simultaneous infinite series leads to a contradiction—something cannot be both inside and outside a totality. In what area of your life are you trying to hold contradictory beliefs (e.g., believing in both absolute freedom and complete determinism)? How will you resolve this?
?
If every temporal event depends on a prior event without a first, does this unsettle your sense of independence? Are you willing to admit that you and all you do are part of a chain that ultimately depends on the Eternal? How should this change your attitude of self-sufficiency?

Resolving the Objection: Non-Being as Cause?
Sanusi answers the objection that the regress would make non-being a cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection of non-being causing existence is resolved by distinguishing between efficient cause and condition.

Core Idea
Temporal events are not causes; they are conditions for the Eternal Cause to act.

After concluding that the series of causes is sequential without a first, an objection arises: doesn't this make a non-existent thing a cause? Sanusi clarifies that the true efficient cause is the Eternal, and temporal events are merely conditions. This lesson explores that crucial distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic and translation. The objector claims that if each event depends on a previous one, then a previous non-existent event would cause the next, which is absurd.
2
Grasp the answer
Sanusi denies the claim. He distinguishes between the efficient cause (the Eternal) and a condition (temporal events).
The temporal events are syurūṭ (conditions), not causes.
3
Reflect on the significance
This preserves the argument: the true cause is always existent, and the regress is not a causal regress but a chain of conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : إن على هذا التقدير يجب إسناد كل حادث إلى حادث آخر قبله فهذه الأسباب التي لا نهاية لها ، إما أن توجد دفعة واحدة ، أو يكون كل متأخر مستنداً إلى ما كان متقدماً عليه ، لا إلى نهاية . والقسم الأول باطل لوجوه : الأول : إن القول بوجود أسباب ومسببات لا نهاية لها دفعة واحدة : قول باطل على ما سبق في هذا الكتاب تقريره . والثاني : إن على التقدير بكون مجموع تلك الأشياء ، إنما حدث في ذلك الوقت ، فيعود السؤال في أن حدوث ذلك المجموع في ذلك الوقت المعين ، لا بد له من سبب حادث ، وذلك باطل ، لأن ذلك السبب ، لكونه سبباً للمجموع الحوادث ، يجب أن يكون مغايراً لذلك المجموع. ولكونه حادثاً يكون داخلا في مجموع الحوادث ، فيكون الشيء الواحد ، داخلا في ذلك المجموع ، وخارجاً عنه . وهو محال . والثالث : إن الحس يدل على أن أجزاء الحركة ما حصلت على الإجتماع دفعة واحدة ، والأشخاص التي دخلت في الوجود بأسرها لم توجد مجتمعة في هذا الزمان المعين. ولما ثبت : أن كل حادث ، فلا بد من إستناده إلى حادث آخر ، لا إلى نهاية . وثبت : أن تلك الأسباب والمسببات التي لا نهاية لها ، لم توجد دفعة : وجب أن يقال : إن كل واحد منها استند إلى ما قبله لا إلى أول . وذلك هو المطلوب . فإن قالوا : فعلى هذا التقدير ، يلزم أن يكون المعدوم علة للموجود . فنقول : ليس الأمر كذلك ، بل العلة المؤثرة في وجود المعلول : هو الموجود القديم الدائم، وكل واحد من هذه الحوادث فإنه شرط لكون ذلك القديم مؤثراً في وجود الحادث المتأخر ، على ما سبق تقريره .
The second: On this assumption, every temporal event must be attributed to another temporal event before it. So these causes that have no end are either found all at once, or each later one depends on what was before it without end. The first division is false for reasons: The first: The statement that there exist causes and effects without end all at once is false, as previously established in this book. The second: On the assumption that the totality of those things occurred at that time, the question returns: the occurrence of that totality at that specific time must have a temporal cause, and this is false, because that cause, being a cause for the totality of events, must be distinct from that totality. And being temporal, it would be included in that totality of events, so a single thing would be both inside and outside that totality, which is impossible. The third: Sensory evidence indicates that parts of motion do not occur simultaneously, and all individuals that came into existence did not exist together at that specified time. And since it is established that every temporal event must rely on another temporal event without end, and it is established that those causes and effects without end did not exist at once, it must be said that each one of them relies on what is before it without a first. That is the required conclusion. If they say: On this assumption, it would follow that non-being is a cause for being. We say: It is not so. Rather, the efficient cause in the existence of the effect is the Eternal Existing One, and each one of these temporal events is a condition for that Eternal One to be efficient in the existence of the later temporal event, as previously established.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage is the second objection against the claim that the first temporal event requires a temporal cause. If that were true, it would entail an infinite regress of causes. The author analyzes whether this infinite series exists simultaneously or sequentially. He first refutes simultaneous existence with three arguments: (1) the falsity of a actually infinite set of simultaneous temporal entities, as shown earlier; (2) the totality of such events, if they occurred at once, would itself need a temporal cause, leading to a circularity where the cause is both part of and outside the totality; (3) sensory evidence shows that parts of motion and individuals do not come into existence all at once. Then he argues that since simultaneous infinite is impossible, the only option is sequential dependency without a first term. This is the required conclusion. An objection is raised: this seems to make a non-existent thing a cause. The author answers by distinguishing between the true cause (the Eternal) and temporal events being mere conditions (شروط) for God's bringing about later events. This preserves the soundness of the argument.

قديم دائم
شرط

Correction: The passage explains that only the Eternal is the efficient cause; temporal events are conditions, not causes.

Key Takeaway
Temporal events are conditions, not causes; the only true efficient cause is the Eternal.

Why Temporality Cannot Be the Essence of a Body
Refuting that temporality is identical to essence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author rejects that a body's temporality could be identical to its essence, using the concept of persistence.

Core Idea
If temporality were your essence, you'd vanish the next moment.

This passage presents a refined argument against the idea that bodies are temporal. It considers two logical possibilities for how temporality relates to a body's essence. The first possibility—that temporality is the essence itself—is shown to lead to an absurd consequence: the body would be unable to persist. This forces the opponent to accept that temporality must be additional, which the author then refutes elsewhere.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text once, then the translation. Notice the structure: the author states the two possibilities, then focuses on the first one.
The passage is compact; every word matters.
2
Identify the two divisions
The author says temporality must be either identical to the essence or additional. This is a standard logical division. Note that only the first division is refuted here.
The second division is assumed to be refuted elsewhere.
3
Understand the meaning of 'temporality'
The author defines temporality as 'coming out from non-existence into existence.' This is crucial: it is an event, not a permanent state.
This definition is used to show that a body after its first moment is not 'temporal' in that sense.
4
Follow the logical consequence
If temporality equals essence, then in the second moment, when temporality (the event) is no longer present, the essence (being identical) must also be absent. Hence the body would vanish.
The argument hinges on the inseparability of essence and attribute if they are identical.
5
Appreciate the conclusion
The author concludes that such a body would be impossible of persistence, which is false. Therefore temporality cannot be identical to essence.
This is a proof by contradiction (burhan al-tamani).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : أن نقول : لو كان الجسم حادثاً ، لكان حدوثه إما أن يكون عين ذاته، أو زائد عليه . والقسمان باطلان فالقول بحدوث : باطل . إنما قلنا : إنه يمتنع أن يكون حدوثه نفس ذاته لوجهين : الأول : أن المراد من الحدوث خروجه من العدم إلى الوجود ، فالجسم حال بقائه ليس حادثاً بهذا التفسير ، فلو كان حدوثه عين ذاته . ثم إن الحدوث غير حاصل في الزمان الثاني، وجب أن لا تبقى ذاته في الزمان الثاني . فهذا يقتضي أن يكون الجسم ممتنع البقاء . وذلك باطل
The second proof: We say: If the body were temporal, its temporality would either be identical to its essence or additional to it. Both divisions are false, so the statement that it is temporal is false. We say: It is impossible for its temporality to be the same as its essence for two reasons: The first: The meaning of temporality is its coming out from non-existence into existence. So the body in the state of its remaining is not temporal according to this interpretation. If its temporality were identical to its essence, then since temporality does not occur in the second moment, its essence would necessarily not remain in the second moment. This would require the body to be impossible of persistence. And that is false.
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage presents a second proof (الحجة الثانية) concerning the temporality of bodies. It considers the logical possibilities for the attribute of temporality if a body were temporal: either it is the very essence of the body or something additional to it. The author claims both are false, concluding that the body cannot be temporal. Only the first division is treated here: if temporality were identical to essence, then since 'temporality' means coming into existence, and the body after the first moment exists but is not 'coming into existence', its essence would have to vanish—otherwise the essence would still be temporal in later moments, which contradicts the definition. Thus the body would be incapable of persistence, which is absurd. The second division (temporality as additional) is not discussed in this window, but the argument appears to be continued elsewhere.

Correction: The argument is dialectical: it refutes a specific claim about temporality being essential, not a positive proof of eternity. The author is showing an internal contradiction in the opponent's position.

Checkpoint

D) Because temporality is an attribute that can be separated from essence.

Key Takeaway
Temporality cannot be identical to the essence of a body because that would imply the body cannot persist beyond the first moment, which is false.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your coming into existence were your essence, you would vanish the moment you were born. But you persist each day—what does that persistence tell you about the One who keeps you in being moment by moment? Are you living as if your existence is a given, or do you thank the Creator for every breath?
?
The author defines temporality strictly as the event of coming from nothing to something. Right now, you are not 'temporal' in that sense—you already exist. Yet you are completely dependent on Allah for your continued existence. How does this realization confront your feeling of self-sufficiency? What would change if you truly believed your existence is renewed each instant?
?
If temporality were your essence, you would not be here reading this. Your persistence is evidence that your essence is not your coming-into-being. What gratitude is due to the One who sustains you? And what would it look like for you to live today in recognition that your entire existence is a gift, not a right?

Third Argument from Sensation: Endless Chain and Non-Existence Objection
The mutakallim presents the third argument from sensation and answers an objection regarding non-existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third argument from sensation for an endless chain of contingent causes and how the mutakallim answers the objection that non-existence would be a cause.

Core Idea
Sensation shows non-simultaneity, so each contingent depends on a prior, safeguarded by an eternal cause.

In this passage, the mutakallim presents the third of the arguments for an endless chain of contingent causes, drawing on sensory evidence that motion and existence are not simultaneous. An objection is then raised: if every contingent depends on a prior, then at the beginning non-existence would cause existence. The answer clarifies that the effective cause is the eternal being, and contingents are merely conditions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the overall argument. Note the flow: sensory premise, inference, conclusion, objection, answer.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the sensory premise
Locate the statement about sensation indicating that parts of motion are not simultaneous and that all existents are not together in one time. Understand why this is crucial.
This is the foundation of the argument.
3
Trace the inference to endless dependence
Follow the logic: since each contingent event must depend on another contingent (no first), and the series is not simultaneous, each depends on the previous—establishing an endless chain.
The conclusion is that there is no first contingent; the chain is beginningless.
4
Analyze the objection and its answer
Read the objection: that this would make non-existence a cause. Then study the answer: the true cause is the eternal existent, and contingents are conditions, not causes in the same sense.
This distinction is key to avoiding absurdity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثالث: إن الحس يدل على أن أجزاء الحركة ما حصلت على الاجتماع دفعة واحدة، والأشخاص التي دخلت في الوجود بأسرها لم توجد مجتمعة في هذا الزمان المعين. ولما ثبت: أن كل حادث، فلا بد من استناده إلى حادث آخر، لا إلى نهاية. وثبت: أن تلك الأسباب والمسببات التي لا نهاية لها، لم توجد دفعة: وجب أن يقال: إن كل واحد منها استند إلى ما قبله لا إلى أول. وذلك هو المطلوب. فإن قالوا: فعلى هذا التقدير، يلزم أن يكون المعدوم علة للموجود. فنقول: ليس الأمر كذلك، بل العلة المؤثرة في وجود المعلول: هو الموجود القديم الدائم، وكل واحد من هذه الحوادث فإنه شرط لكون ذلك القديم مؤثراً في وجود الحادث المتأخر، على ما سبق تقريره.
And the third: that sensation indicates that the parts of motion did not occur simultaneously, and the individuals that came into existence as a whole did not exist together in this specific time. And since it has been established that every contingent event must depend on another contingent event, without end, and it has been established that those causes and effects which have no end did not occur simultaneously, it must be said that each one of them depends on the one before it, not on a first. That is what is required. If they say: on this estimation, it would necessitate that the non-existent is a cause of the existent. We say: the matter is not so; rather, the effective cause in the existence of the effect is the eternal, permanent existent, and each one of these contingent events is a condition for that eternal being to be effective in bringing about the later contingent event, as was previously established.
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage presents a proof (the third argument of a larger discussion) from sensation (al-hiss) for the necessity of an endless chain of contingent causes. The proof begins by observing that parts of motion are not simultaneous and that all individuals that have come into existence are not present together in any given time. From this it is established that every contingent event (hādith) must depend on another contingent event without end (lā ilā nihāyah). Since the series of causes and effects has no end and does not occur all at once, each individual in the series depends on its predecessor, not on a first cause. This is the desired conclusion (al-maṭlūb). An objection is then raised: if each contingent depends on a prior contingent, then at the first step the non-existent (al-ma‘dūm) would be the cause of the existent, which is absurd. The answer is that the effective cause (al-‘illah al-mu’aththirah) is the eternal, permanent existent (al-mawjūd al-qadīm al-dā’im). Each contingent event is not a cause but a condition (sharṭ) for that eternal being to be effective in bringing about the later contingent, as previously established. Thus the objection is deflected.

Key Terms

الحركة
الحادث
السبب
العلة

القديم
الشرط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This proof argues for an actual infinite regress of causes in a single vertical chain, where each cause is independent.
Correction: The proof argues for a beginningless series of contingents that all depend on an eternal cause, not as a cause in the same sense but as a condition. The chain is horizontal and dependent on the eternal.

Checkpoint
According to the third argument, why must each contingent event depend on a prior contingent without end?
A) Because sensation shows that motion occurs in parts and not all at once.
B) Because the universe cannot have a beginning.
C) Because the non-existent cannot be a cause.
D) Because the eternal cause is a condition.

Key Takeaway
The third argument uses sensory non-simultaneity to prove that each contingent depends on a prior, and the objection that non-existence causes existence is resolved by identifying the eternal existent as the true cause and contingents as conditions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

When you examine your daily life, do you truly feel that every moment of your existence depends on the Eternal Being, or do you live as if secondary causes are self-sufficient? How can you cultivate that awareness in small decisions?
?
The objection from non-existence is answered by distinguishing cause from condition. Are there things in your life—people, possessions, habits—that you treat as the real cause of your happiness, when they are merely conditions? What would change if you saw only Allah as the effective cause?
?
If you believed that no contingent event occurs without Allah's effective causation, would you still be anxious about outcomes? What would it mean for your trust in Allah if you internalized this truth?

The Dilemma of Contingency: Identity or Addition?
Author presents second proof and refutes identity of contingency with essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophical dilemma that if bodies are contingent, their contingency must be either identical to their essence or additional to it, and the two arguments refuting the identity option.

Core Idea
Contingency cannot be identical to essence, else bodies would not persist.

In this passage, the mutakallim presents a powerful objection against the contingency of bodies. He argues that if bodies are contingent, their contingency must either be their very essence or an added attribute. He then refutes the identity option with two reasons. This lesson focuses on the dilemma and those two reasons.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Dilemma
Read the opening sentence: contingency is either identical to essence or additional. Note that the author claims both are false.
This sets up the entire argument.
2
Analyze Reason One
Focus on the first reason against identity: if contingency were the essence, the body would not persist beyond the first moment of existence. This contradicts observed reality.
Contingency is defined as transition from non-existence to existence.
3
Analyze Reason Two
The second reason: if contingency were identical to essence, then knowing the essence would give necessary knowledge of contingency, which is not true.
We know bodies exist but their contingency is not self-evident.
4
Reflect on the Conclusion
Since identity is refuted, the author moves to reject addition next. Understand that this is part of a larger refutation.
The lesson covers only the identity part.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية: أن نقول: لو كان الجسم حادثاً، لكان حدوثه إما أن يكون عين ذاته، أو زائد عليه. والقسمان باطلان فالقول بحدوث: باطل. إنما قلنا: إنه يمتنع أن يكون حدوثه نفس ذاته لوجهين: الأول: أن المراد من الحدوث خروجه من العدم إلى الوجود، فالجسم حال بقائه ليس حادثاً بهذا التفسير، فلو كان حدوثه عين ذاته، ثم إن الحدوث غير حاصل في الزمان الثاني، وجب أن لا تبقى ذاته في الزمان الثاني. فهذا يقتضي أن يكون الجسم ممتنع البقاء. وذلك باطل. والثاني: أنه لو كان حدوثه عين ذاته، لكان العالم بذاته عالماً بحدوثه. وذلك يوجب أن يكون العلم بحدوثه ضرورياً، كما أن العلم بوجوده ضروري. وإنما قلنا: إنه يمتنع أن يكون حدوثه زائد عليه، لأنه يلزم أن يكون حدوث ذلك الحادث زائداً عليه، ويلزم التسلسل. فإن قيل: لم لا يجوز أن يقال: حدوث الجسم عين ذاته؟ ويلزم كون الجسم حادثاً حالاً محالاً. وأيضاً فهذا وارد في الصفات والأعراض، وأيضاً: فهذا وارد عليكم في قدم الأجسام على ما سيأتي تقريره في باب دلائل القائلين بالحدوث. والجواب عن الأول: إنه قد اتفق العقلاء المعتبرون على أن الأجسام باقية دائمة. وأما الثاني: فكثير من الخلق التزموا أن بقاء الأعراض محال. وأما الثالث: فهو أن قدم الجسم، لما كان عين ذاته، لم يمتنع أن يقال: إنه قديم في كل الأوقات. أما لو قلنا: إن حدوثه عين ذاته، لزم أن يكون حادثاً في كل الأوقات. وذلك ينافي كونه باقياً مستمر الوجود. فظهر الفرق.
The second proof: that we say: if the body were contingent, then its contingency would either be identical to its essence or additional to it. Both divisions are false, so the statement of contingency is false. We say: it is impossible for its contingency to be identical to its essence for two reasons: First: that by contingency is meant its transition from non-existence to existence, so the body in the state of its subsistence is not contingent according to this interpretation; so if its contingency were identical to its essence, and contingency does not occur in the second time, then its essence would necessarily not persist in the second time. This requires that the body be impossible of persistence. That is false. Second: that if its contingency were identical to its essence, then one who knows the essence would know its contingency. This necessitates that knowledge of its contingency be necessary, just as knowledge of its existence is necessary. And we say: it is impossible for its contingency to be additional to it, because it would necessitate that the contingency of that contingent event be additional to it, and an infinite regress would follow. If it is said: why is it not permissible to say that the contingency of the body is identical to its essence? And it would necessitate that the body be contingent in an impossible state? Also, this applies to attributes and accidents; also, this applies to you in the pre-eternity of bodies, as will come in the chapter on the proofs of those who hold contingency. The answer to the first: that the considered rational people have agreed that bodies are permanent and enduring. As for the second: many people have committed to the impossibility of the persistence of accidents. As for the third: that the pre-eternity of the body, since it is identical to its essence, it is not impossible to say that it is pre-eternal in all times. But if we say that its contingency is identical to its essence, it would necessitate that it be contingent in all times, and that is incompatible with its being permanent, of continuous existence. So the difference appears.
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage presents a philosophical argument (al-hujjah al-thāniyah) against the contingency of bodies and then refutes it. The argument states: if a body were contingent (hādith), its contingency (ḥudūth) must be either identical to its essence (ʿayn dhātih) or additional (zā’id) to it. Both options are claimed to be false, therefore the body cannot be contingent. Two reasons are given for rejecting the identity option: first, contingency is defined as the transition from non-existence to existence; in the second moment the body is no longer in transition, so if contingency were identical to essence, the essence would cease to exist in the second moment, making persistence impossible. Second, if contingency were identical to essence, knowledge of the essence would give necessary knowledge of its contingency, which is false. Then the possibility of contingency being additional is rejected because it leads to an infinite regress (tasalsul). An objection follows: why can't contingency be identical to essence? The objector raises difficulties about the state of the body and also points out that similar reasoning applies to attributes and accidents, and that the opponent's own view on pre-eternity faces problems. The author then provides three answers: (1) rational people agree that bodies are permanent (bāqiyah dā’imah), so the first argument fails; (2) many hold that accidents cannot persist (baqā’ al-aʿrāḍ muḥāl), so the problem of persistence is avoided; (3) if the body's pre-eternity (qidam) is identical to its essence, it can be called pre-eternal at all times, but if contingency were identical to essence, the body would be contingent at all times, which contradicts its permanence. Thus the difference (farq) between the two cases is clarified.

Key Terms
الجسم
الحدوث
الذات
البقاء
العلم الضروري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If contingency is identical to essence, the body would be constantly contingent.
Correction: Actually, if identical, when contingency ends (in the second moment), the essence would cease, so the body would not persist.

Checkpoint
According to the first reason, what would happen to the body in the second moment if its contingency were identical to its essence?
A) It would remain the same.
B) It would cease to exist.
C) It would become eternal.
D) It would be contingent again.

Key Takeaway
Contingency cannot be identical to essence because it would imply non-persistence and necessary knowledge that contradicts observed reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own existence, do you treat it as something permanent or as a gift that could vanish at any moment? The author argues that if contingency were identical to essence, the body would not persist. How does this challenge your assumption about your own stability, and what should change in your daily attitude toward life?
?
Have you ever assumed that knowing something's existence automatically gives you knowledge of its origin? The second reason shows this is not necessary. In your life, do you often rush to conclusions about others' actions without understanding their true nature? How can you pause and reflect on the deeper reality of things?
?
The dilemma forces a choice: either contingency is essential to you or separate. Which option do you unconsciously live by, and how does that affect your reliance on Allah? If you lived as if your existence were purely dependent every moment, would your priorities shift? Commit to one concrete change today that reflects your contingency.

Refuting Addition and Answering Objections
Author refutes addition and answers three objections to the identity view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why contingency cannot be an additional attribute (leading to infinite regress) and how the mutakallim answers objections from the opponent.

Core Idea
If contingency were an added attribute, an infinite regress would follow.

After refuting the identity option, the mutakallim now turns to the possibility that contingency is an additional attribute to the essence. He argues that this leads to an infinite regress, which is impossible. Then he addresses objections that attempt to revive the identity view, giving three responses that clarify the difference between contingency and pre-eternity.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Regress Argument
Read why addition is impossible: it would require that the contingency of that added attribute itself be a new attribute, leading to infinite chain.
This is a classic infinite regress (tasalsul).
2
Identify the Objections
The opponent raises three points: (1) identity would make body contingent in an impossible state; (2) same reasoning applies to attributes/accidents; (3) it applies to opponent's view on pre-eternity.
The objections challenge the earlier refutation of identity.
3
Examine the Answers
Read each answer: (1) rational people agree bodies persist; (2) many deny accident persistence; (3) pre-eternity as essence is different from contingency as essence.
The third answer shows a crucial distinction.
4
Synthesize the Difference
Contrast the two cases: pre-eternity identical to essence allows eternal existence at all times, but contingency identical to essence would require constant contingency, contradicting permanence.
This clarifies why the objection fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية: أن نقول: لو كان الجسم حادثاً، لكان حدوثه إما أن يكون عين ذاته، أو زائد عليه. والقسمان باطلان فالقول بحدوث: باطل. إنما قلنا: إنه يمتنع أن يكون حدوثه نفس ذاته لوجهين: الأول: أن المراد من الحدوث خروجه من العدم إلى الوجود، فالجسم حال بقائه ليس حادثاً بهذا التفسير، فلو كان حدوثه عين ذاته، ثم إن الحدوث غير حاصل في الزمان الثاني، وجب أن لا تبقى ذاته في الزمان الثاني. فهذا يقتضي أن يكون الجسم ممتنع البقاء. وذلك باطل. والثاني: أنه لو كان حدوثه عين ذاته، لكان العالم بذاته عالماً بحدوثه. وذلك يوجب أن يكون العلم بحدوثه ضرورياً، كما أن العلم بوجوده ضروري. وإنما قلنا: إنه يمتنع أن يكون حدوثه زائد عليه، لأنه يلزم أن يكون حدوث ذلك الحادث زائداً عليه، ويلزم التسلسل. فإن قيل: لم لا يجوز أن يقال: حدوث الجسم عين ذاته؟ ويلزم كون الجسم حادثاً حالاً محالاً. وأيضاً فهذا وارد في الصفات والأعراض، وأيضاً: فهذا وارد عليكم في قدم الأجسام على ما سيأتي تقريره في باب دلائل القائلين بالحدوث. والجواب عن الأول: إنه قد اتفق العقلاء المعتبرون على أن الأجسام باقية دائمة. وأما الثاني: فكثير من الخلق التزموا أن بقاء الأعراض محال. وأما الثالث: فهو أن قدم الجسم، لما كان عين ذاته، لم يمتنع أن يقال: إنه قديم في كل الأوقات. أما لو قلنا: إن حدوثه عين ذاته، لزم أن يكون حادثاً في كل الأوقات. وذلك ينافي كونه باقياً مستمر الوجود. فظهر الفرق.
The second proof: that we say: if the body were contingent, then its contingency would either be identical to its essence or additional to it. Both divisions are false, so the statement of contingency is false. We say: it is impossible for its contingency to be identical to its essence for two reasons: First: that by contingency is meant its transition from non-existence to existence, so the body in the state of its subsistence is not contingent according to this interpretation; so if its contingency were identical to its essence, and contingency does not occur in the second time, then its essence would necessarily not persist in the second time. This requires that the body be impossible of persistence. That is false. Second: that if its contingency were identical to its essence, then one who knows the essence would know its contingency. This necessitates that knowledge of its contingency be necessary, just as knowledge of its existence is necessary. And we say: it is impossible for its contingency to be additional to it, because it would necessitate that the contingency of that contingent event be additional to it, and an infinite regress would follow. If it is said: why is it not permissible to say that the contingency of the body is identical to its essence? And it would necessitate that the body be contingent in an impossible state? Also, this applies to attributes and accidents; also, this applies to you in the pre-eternity of bodies, as will come in the chapter on the proofs of those who hold contingency. The answer to the first: that the considered rational people have agreed that bodies are permanent and enduring. As for the second: many people have committed to the impossibility of the persistence of accidents. As for the third: that the pre-eternity of the body, since it is identical to its essence, it is not impossible to say that it is pre-eternal in all times. But if we say that its contingency is identical to its essence, it would necessitate that it be contingent in all times, and that is incompatible with its being permanent, of continuous existence. So the difference appears.
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage presents a philosophical argument (al-hujjah al-thāniyah) against the contingency of bodies and then refutes it. The argument states: if a body were contingent (hādith), its contingency (ḥudūth) must be either identical to its essence (ʿayn dhātih) or additional (zā’id) to it. Both options are claimed to be false, therefore the body cannot be contingent. Two reasons are given for rejecting the identity option: first, contingency is defined as the transition from non-existence to existence; in the second moment the body is no longer in transition, so if contingency were identical to essence, the essence would cease to exist in the second moment, making persistence impossible. Second, if contingency were identical to essence, knowledge of the essence would give necessary knowledge of its contingency, which is false. Then the possibility of contingency being additional is rejected because it leads to an infinite regress (tasalsul). An objection follows: why can't contingency be identical to essence? The objector raises difficulties about the state of the body and also points out that similar reasoning applies to attributes and accidents, and that the opponent's own view on pre-eternity faces problems. The author then provides three answers: (1) rational people agree that bodies are permanent (bāqiyah dā’imah), so the first argument fails; (2) many hold that accidents cannot persist (baqā’ al-aʿrāḍ muḥāl), so the problem of persistence is avoided; (3) if the body's pre-eternity (qidam) is identical to its essence, it can be called pre-eternal at all times, but if contingency were identical to essence, the body would be contingent at all times, which contradicts its permanence. Thus the difference (farq) between the two cases is clarified.

Key Terms
الزائد
التسلسل
البقاء
العرض
القدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The infinite regress argument is the only reason against contingency.
Correction: The author also refutes identity, and the objection-answer section clarifies further.

Checkpoint
What is the consequence if contingency were an additional attribute?
A) It would make the body eternal.
B) It would lead to infinite regress.
C) It would make knowledge necessary.
D) It would negate persistence.

Key Takeaway
Contingency cannot be additional because it leads to infinite regress, and the objections against the identity view are answered by distinguishing between contingency and pre-eternity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

You just read that your very existence is contingent at every moment—it is not an added feature but your very reality. When you think about your life’s achievements and failures, do you truly see yourself as nothing without God sustaining you each instant, or do you secretly believe you have some independent existence? What would it mean to live each moment in that awareness?
?
The author argues that if contingency were added, an infinite regress would follow. In your own spiritual life, do you find yourself adding ‘layers’ of justifications to your actions, leading to endless excuses before God? How does the simplicity of accepting your total dependence cut through that cycle?
?
The lesson shows that even persistence (baqā') is not self-sustained—it is given. Do you take your continued faith and good deeds for granted, assuming they will persist on their own? What might change if you thanked God daily for keeping you in existence and in faith?

Objection: Huduth Identical to Dhat — and Its Refutation
Author presents and refutes the objection that huduth is identical to dhat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that a body's occurrence could be identical to its essence and the three arguments refuting it.

Core Idea
If occurrence were identical to essence, a body would necessarily know its own occurrence—but that is absurd.

This passage tackles a subtle objection: could the temporal occurrence of a body be the very same as its essence? The author argues no, and provides three distinct refutations to clarify the relationship between essence and accident. Understanding this argument helps solidify the distinction between essential attributes and accidental ones in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and its implication
Read the first part of the passage: the objection that if huduth is identical to dhat, then the world would know its own occurrence necessarily. Note why this is problematic.
The author does not accept that knowledge of occurrence is necessary.
2
Understand the regress argument
Follow the argument that if occurrence were an additional attribute, each occurrence would need another occurrence, leading to infinite regress.
This shows why occurrence cannot be a separate added attribute either.
3
Examine the three answers
Read the three answers the author gives against the hypothetical objection that occurrence could be identical. Identify the core point of each answer.
Answers: (1) consensus on persistence, (2) accidents don't persist, (3) eternal vs. occurring bodies.
4
Reflect on the difference established
Consider how the author concludes that the difference between essence and accident is clear. Summarize why occurrence cannot be identical to essence.
This reinforces the distinction between essential and accidental attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني : أنه لو كان حدوثه عين ذاته ، لكان العالم بذاته عالماً بحدوثه . وذلك يوجب أن يكون العلم بحدوثه ضرورياً ، كما أن العلم بوجوده ضروري. وإنما قلنا : إنه يمتنع أن يكون حدوثه زائد عليه ، لأنه يلزم أن يكون حدوث ذلك الحادث زائداً عليه ، ويلزم التسلسل . فإن قيل : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : حدوث الجسم عين ذاته ؟ ويلزم كون الجسم حادثاً، حالا محالاً وأيضاً فهذا وارد في الصفات والأعراض وأيضاً : فهذا وارد عليكم في قدم : الأجسام على ما سيأتي تقريره في باب دلائل القائلين بالحدوث والجواب عن الأول : إنه قد اتفق العقلاء المعتبرون على أن الأجسام باقية دائمة . وأما الثاني : فكثير من الخلق، التزموا أن بقاء : الأعراض محال وأما الثالث : فهو أن قدم الجسم ، لما كان عين ذاته ، لم يمتنع أن يقال : إنه قديم في كل الأوقات. أما لو قلنا: إن حدوثه عين ذاته، لزم أن يكون حادثاً في كل الأوقات. وذلك ينافي كونه ،باقياً ، مستمر الوجود . فظهر الفرق .
The second [objection]: If its occurrence were identical to its essence, then the world would by its essence be knowing its occurrence. That would necessitate that knowledge of its occurrence be necessary, just as knowledge of its existence is necessary. We only say: It is impossible for its occurrence to be additional to it, because it would necessitate that the occurrence of that occurrence be additional to it, and it would necessitate infinite regress. If it is said: Why is it not permissible to say that the occurrence of the body is identical to its essence? And it would necessitate that the body be occurring, which is absurd. Also, this applies to attributes and accidents. Also, this applies against you regarding the eternity of bodies, as will be discussed in the chapter on the proofs of those who affirm occurrence. The answer to the first: The rational people have agreed that bodies are permanent and enduring. As for the second: Many creatures have committed to the impossibility of the persistence of accidents. As for the third: The eternity of the body, since it was identical to its essence, it is not impossible to say that it is eternal at all times. But if we say that its occurrence is identical to its essence, it would necessitate that it be occurring at all times. And that contradicts its being permanent, continuous in existence. So the difference is clear.

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its refutation. The objection claims that if a body's occurrence (huduth) were identical to its essence (dhat), then the body would necessarily have self-knowledge of its own occurrence, just as it knows its own existence. But this is false. The author first explains why occurrence cannot be an additional attribute—if it were, it would lead to infinite regress. Then he considers a hypothetical objection: why not say occurrence is identical to essence? That would imply the body is always occurring, which is impossible for a persistent body. The author gives three answers: (1) rational people agree bodies persist; (2) persistence of accidents is often considered impossible; (3) if a body is eternal, its essence is eternal, so it is eternal at all times, but if occurrence were identical to essence, it would be occurring at all times, contradicting its persistence. Thus the difference between essence and accident is clear.

The Third Proof: Two Stations and the First Argument for Original State
Razi introduces the third proof, distinguishes two stations, and gives the first argument for the principle of original state.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the structure of Razi's third proof, the distinction between probability and certainty, and the first argument for the principle that the original state of everything is persistence.

Core Idea
The persistent is independent; the occurring depends: persistence is more likely.

Razi presents a third proof for the occurrence of bodies, but he first clarifies his method. He distinguishes between seeking probability and certainty, then offers a rational argument for the foundational principle that things remain as they are until proven otherwise. This argument grounds the principle in the nature of independence and preponderance.

Guided Path
1
Understand the historical context and two stations
Read how earlier scholars argued from observed regularity, and how theologians criticized them. Then note Razi's two stations: one for probability, one for certainty.
This is a methodological distinction often missed.
2
Grasp the principle of original state (al-asl baqa')
The principle says that the original state of everything is its remaining as it was, unless evidence of change appears. Razi needs to justify this principle.
This principle is used in many arguments.
3
Follow the first argument for the principle
Trace the reasoning: a persistent thing is independent of an effect; an occurring thing depends. Independence implies preponderance in existence.
The argument uses the notion of 'preponderator' (murajjih) to show impossibility otherwise.
4
Connect to probability in opinion
Razi concludes that since persistence is more preponderant in reality, a true opinion (zann) must reflect that preponderance, making persistence more likely.
This supports the first station of probability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : اعلم أن المتقدمين كانوا يقولون : ما شاهدنا ليلا ، إلا وقبله نهاراً ، ولا نهاراً إلا وقبله ليل . فوجب أن يكون الأمر كذلك . والمتكلمون شنعوا عليهم ، وقالوا : هذا هذا جمع بين الشاهد والغائب بمحض التحكم ، وأنه باطل واعلم أن القوم لهم ههنا مقامان : المقام الأول : إنا لا نتمسك بهذا الدليل، في إثبات القطع والجزم بهذا القول بل في إثبات أن هذا القول هو الأولى والأقرب والأخلق بالقبول . المقام الثاني : أن نتمسك بهذا الطريق في إثبات القطع والجزم أما المقام الأول : فتقريره : أن الأصل في كل أمر بقاؤه على ما كان فإن قام دليل منفصل على وقوع التغير، قضينا به. وإلا فالواجب هو الحكم ببقائه على ما كان فيفتقر ههنا إلى بيان أمرين : إلى بيان أن الأصل في كل أمر بقاؤه على ما كان . ثم إلى بيان أنه لما كان الأمر كذلك ، لزم القول بثبوت هذا المطلوب أما المقام الأول : وهو بيان أن الأصل في كل أمر بقاؤه على ما كان فيدل عليه وجوه : الأول : أن الباقي حال بقائه غني عن المؤثر ، وإلا لزم تكون الكائن والحادث حال حدوثه مفتقر إلى المؤثر. وهو متفق عليه والغني عن المؤثر راجح الوجود ، بالنسبة إلى المحتاج إلى المؤثر : لأن الغنى إن لم يكن راجح الوجود لزم أن يكون إما مساوياً ، أو مرجوحاً . وعلى التقديرين فكان يلزم افتقاره إلى المرجح . مع أنا فرضناه غنياً عن المؤثر . هذا خلف . وأما المحتاج إلى المؤثر . فيلزم أن يكون المحتاج إلى المؤثر غنياً عنه . هذا خلف . وإذا ثبت كون الباقي راجحاً على الحادث في نفس الأمر ، وجب أن يكون كذلك في الظن الصادق ، حتى يكون الظن مطابقا للمظنون .
The third proof: Know that the early scholars used to say: We have not observed a night except that before it was day, nor a day except that before it was night. So it must be that the matter is thus. The theologians criticized them, saying: This is an analogy between the visible and the unseen by sheer arbitrariness, and it is false. And know that these people have two stations: The first station: We do not use this proof to establish certainty and conviction regarding this statement, but rather to establish that this statement is the most preferable, nearest, and most worthy of acceptance. The second station: That we use this method to establish certainty and conviction. As for the first station: Its explanation is that the original state in every matter is its remaining as it was. If a separate evidence arises for change, we judge by it. Otherwise, it is obligatory to judge its remaining as it was. So here we need to establish two things: To establish that the original state in every matter is its remaining as it was. Then to establish that since the matter is thus, it necessitates affirming this required conclusion. As for the first station, which is establishing that the original state in every matter is its remaining as it was, it is indicated by aspects: The first: That the persistent thing, in the state of its persistence, is independent of the effect; otherwise the coming-to-be and occurring thing would, in the state of its occurrence, be dependent on the effect. And this is agreed upon. The independent-of-effect is preponderant in existence relative to the one dependent on the effect. Because if independent were not preponderant in existence, it would have to be either equal or deficient. In both cases, it would necessitate its need for a preponderator, despite our assumption that it is independent of the effect. This is an absurdity. As for the one dependent on the effect, it would necessitate that the dependent on the effect be independent of it. This is an absurdity. And when it is established that the persistent is preponderant over the occurring in reality, it must be so in true opinion, so that opinion may correspond to the thing opined.
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof for the occurrence of bodies. The author first notes that earlier scholars argued from observed regularity (night following day) to necessary continuity, but theologians dismissed this as arbitrary analogy. The author then clarifies his method: He does not use this proof for certainty but to show probability (first station). The second station (not detailed here) aims for certainty. For the first station, he establishes the principle: 'The original state of everything is its remaining as it was' (al-asl baqa'). Change requires evidence. To justify this principle, he gives the first argument: A persisting thing is independent of an effect (mu'aththir), while an occurring thing depends on one. Independence implies preponderance in existence; otherwise an independent thing would need a preponderator, which is contradictory. Therefore, persistence is more likely, and a true judgment should reflect this preponderance.

Key Terms
أصل (asl)
بقاء (baqa')
غني عن المؤثر (ghani 'an al-mu'aththir)
محتاج (muhtaj)
راجح (rajih)
مرجوح (marjuh)
مرجح (murajjih)
ظن (zann)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The principle of original state (al-asl baqa') is an arbitrary assumption with no rational basis.
Correction: Razi grounds it in a rational argument: the persistent is independent of an effect, which makes it preponderant in existence, so it is more likely to continue.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's first argument, why is the persistent thing considered more preponderant in existence than the occurring thing?
A) Because the persistent thing is always observed to continue.
B) Because the persistent thing is independent of an effect, while the occurring depends.
C) Because the occurring thing is more common.
D) Because the principle of original state is self-evident.

Key Takeaway
Razi's first argument establishes the principle of original state by showing that the persistent is independent of an effect and therefore more likely to continue, supporting a probabilistic judgment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own faith, do you assume it will persist unless you deliberately neglect it? How does Razi's principle that the persistent is independent and preponderant challenge you to actively maintain your iman rather than rely on its automatic continuation? Ask yourself honestly: What evidence of change have you ignored because you assumed persistence?
?
Razi says the persistent is more likely to continue than to change. But this is only probability, not certainty. Does your daily life reflect that you truly accept that Allah could change your circumstances at any moment? Or do you live as if the current state of your health, provision, and relationships is guaranteed to persist? Tonight, before sleeping, reflect on one area where you have assumed persistence and recommit to seeing it as dependent on Allah's will.
?
If the persistent is independent of an effect, then it is worth more in reality. What in your life do you consider most 'persistent'—your wealth, your status, your relationships? Are you investing in those things as if they are 'independent'? Or do you recognize that everything besides Allah is dependent and changing? Let this lesson move you to attach your heart to the Only Persistent—Allah—and to treat all else as passing.

Refuting Aristotle's Argument for the Eternity of Time
The author exposes the dependency of Aristotle's followers' argument on their definition of time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the definition of time as a measure of motion leads to a fallacious inference, and how the correct view breaks that chain.

Core Idea
Time's eternity does not prove motion's eternity unless time is defined as its measure.

This passage presents a methodological rebuttal to a famous Aristotelian argument for the eternity of the universe. The author shows that the inference from time's eternity to motion's eternity depends entirely on a specific definition of time. Once we define time correctly, the inference collapses.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the logical flow from the Peripatetic claim to the author's refutation.
The passage is dense but short.
2
Identify the three steps of the argument
The Peripatetics argue: (1) time is eternal, (2) time is the measure of motion, so (3) motion is eternal, and therefore the body (universe) is eternal. Find these in the text.
3
Understand the role of definition
The inference depends on the definition of time as a measure of motion. Ask yourself: if time were not defined that way, would the inference hold?
This is the key logical move.
4
See how the author refutes the argument
The author offers an alternative: time and duration are a self-subsistent substance, not a concomitant of motion. Under this definition, eternity of time does not imply eternity of motion or body.
The refutation is logical, not empirical.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن أصحاب ( أرسطاطاليس ، يحتجون على قدم الزمان . ثم لما كان مذهبهم : أن الزمان مقدار الحركة ، لا جرم أمكنهم أن يستدلوا بقدم الزمان على قدم الحركة ، وبقدم الحركة على قدم الجسم . فإذا قلنا المدة والزمان : جوهر قائم بنفسه ، وأنه ليس من لواحق الحركة ، فحينئذ لا يمكن الاستدلال بقدم المدة على قدم الحركة والجسم (1) . فلتكن هذه الدقيقة معلومة
And know that the followers of Aristotle argue for the eternity of time. Then, since their doctrine is that time is the measure of motion, it is necessarily possible for them to infer from the eternity of time the eternity of motion, and from the eternity of motion the eternity of body. So if we say that duration and time are a self-subsistent substance, and that they are not concomitants of motion, then it is not possible to infer from the eternity of duration the eternity of motion and body. Let this subtle point be known.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage is a methodological note about a proof used by Aristotle's followers. The author states that the Peripatetics argue for the eternity of time. Because they define time as the measure of motion, they can deduce the eternity of motion from the eternity of time, and from motion they deduce the eternity of the body (the physical universe). However, the author points out that if we adopt the correct view—that duration and time are a self-subsistent substance (jawhar qa'im bi nafsihi) and not accidents of motion—then this chain of inference is broken. The eternity of duration does not prove the eternity of motion or body. The author marks this as a subtle but crucial point.

Key Terms
أرسطاطاليس
الزمان
الحركة
الجسم
جوهر قائم بنفسه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If time is eternal, then the universe must be eternal.
Correction: This inference only holds if time is defined as the measure of motion. If time is a self-subsistent substance, its eternity does not entail the eternity of motion or the physical world.

Checkpoint
Why can Aristotle's followers infer the eternity of motion from the eternity of time?
A) Because they believe time is a self-subsistent substance
B) Because they define time as the measure of motion
C) Because they consider time and motion identical
D) Because they assume the eternity of the body

Key Takeaway
The validity of a theological argument often hinges on the definitions of its key terms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you assume that something is eternal or unchanging, do you ever stop to question whether your assumption is based on a definition you accepted without examination? What in your life have you treated as permanent simply because you never considered that it could be otherwise?
?
This lesson shows that a subtle shift in definition can collapse an entire argument. Are there beliefs you hold about God, the world, or yourself that depend on definitions you haven't examined? Will you examine them today, even if it is uncomfortable?
?
If time is a created substance rather than an accident, what does that mean for your own sense of urgency and your relationship with the eternal? Are you living as if time is merely a measure of your own motion, or as a created reality that points to its Creator? How will you change your schedule this week to reflect that truth?

God's Priority is Causal, Not Temporal
The author answers the objection that an agent prior to time cannot be temporally prior.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between temporal priority and causal priority in relation to God and time.

Core Idea
God's priority over creation is causal, not temporal.

This lesson addresses a key objection concerning God's relationship to time. The objection claims that since God is prior to time, His priority cannot be temporal. The author provides a concise yet profound response: God's priority is one of causation, a distinct kind of precedence that does not involve time.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the objection and response.
Notice the concise style of the author's answer.
2
Identify the objection and the answer
The objection is presented in the first part (beginning with 'أما قوله ثانياً'). The answer is the author's statement starting with 'قلنا'.
3
Reflect on the distinction
Consider why causal priority is different from temporal priority. The cause is prior to its effect in essence, not in time, especially when the cause is the Creator of time itself.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما قوله ثانياً : « مجموع الزمان له فاعل مختار، والفاعل المختار متقدم على فعله ، والمتقدم على الزمان ، لا يكون (1) تقدمه بالزمان : قلنا : المؤثر متقدم على الأثر ، تقدماً بالتأثير
As for his second statement: 'The totality of time has a free agent, and the free agent is prior to his act, and that which is prior to time cannot have its priority by time.' We say: The cause (al-mu'aththir) is prior to the effect (al-athar) by a priority of causation (taqaddum bi al-ta'thir).
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage is the author's answer to the objection that since the free agent of time is prior to his act, and this agent is prior to time itself, his priority cannot be temporal. Therefore, the objection goes, God's priority over the world is not by duration. The author responds succinctly: The cause (al-mu'aththir) is prior to the effect (al-athar) by a priority of causation (taqaddum bi al-ta'thir). This is a non-temporal priority, distinct from temporal priority. So the objection is invalid because it mistakenly assumes that all priority must be temporal. The author affirms that God's priority is causal, which is a different kind of precedence.

التقدم بالتأثير
المؤثر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's priority over creation is temporal, like one event before another.
Correction: God's priority is causal, not temporal; He is the cause of time itself.

Key Takeaway
God's priority is causal, not temporal, meaning He is the cause of time, not prior within it.

What one change will you make today to honor Him as the Creator of your time?
?
The objection in the passage assumes that temporal priority is the only kind of priority. Where in your own thinking do you mistakenly assume that God must fit into your timeline—that He should act when you expect, or that He is 'late'? Repent of that assumption now and commit to trusting His causal priority over your temporal expectations.

Causal vs. Temporal Priority
Sanusi distinguishes causal priority from temporal priority in response to an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the priority of the free agent over his act is causal, not temporal.

Core Idea
The agent's priority is by influence, not by time.

Sanusi addresses an objection that the free agent must be temporally prior to his act. The objector argues that since the agent is prior to time itself, temporal priority is impossible. Sanusi clarifies that the priority is one of causality and influence, not duration.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice that the objector presents a dilemma about priority, and Sanusi responds by distinguishing types of priority.
The objection is a common philosophical challenge.
2
Understand the distinction
Focus on Sanusi's key phrase: 'priority of influence and causality' (التقدم بالتأثير والعلية). This is the core of the answer.
Contrast this with temporal priority (التقدم بالزمان).
3
Apply the concept
Reflect on why this distinction matters: It allows God, as the free agent of time, to be causally prior to creation without being bounded by time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما قوله ثانياً : « مجموع الزمان له فاعل مختار، والفاعل المختار متقدم على فعله ، والمتقدم على الزمان ، لا يكون (1) تقدمه بالزمان : قلنا : المؤثر متقدم على الأثر ، تقدماً بالتأثير والعلية. فأما ثبوت تقدمه بوجه آخر، سوى هذا الوجه : فممنوع .
As for his second saying: 'The totality of time has a free agent, and the free agent is prior to his act, and what is prior to time cannot be prior by time.' We said: The efficient cause is prior to the effect, by priority of influence and causality. As for establishing his priority by another mode besides this mode, it is prohibited.
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage is another objection-and-answer. The objector argues that the free agent (al-fa'il al-mukhtar) of time is prior to his act, and since he is prior to time itself, his priority cannot be temporal. The answer distinguishes: the priority is by influence and causality (ta'thir and 'illiyya), not by duration. The objector's claim that the agent is prior to time is not granted in any other sense; only causal priority is affirmed.

العلية
المؤثر
الأثر

Correction: In kalam, priority can be causal (by influence) and does not imply time.

Key Takeaway
The free agent's priority over his act is causal, not temporal.

Distinguishing Possibility in Itself from Absolute Possibility
Sanusi answers an objection by distinguishing possibility in itself from absolute possibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why something that is possible in itself may still be impossible in absolute terms due to external causes, and how this distinction resolves the objection about time ceasing.

Core Idea
Possibility in itself does not guarantee absolute possibility; external causes matter.

Sanusi encounters an objection: if the present moment perishes and nothing follows, time would be cut off. This seems to imply time is not eternal. But Sanusi shows that the objection confuses two kinds of possibility. Let's see how he distinguishes them and why it matters for understanding time and causality.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then its translation. Notice the structure: an objection followed by an answer.
The objection is quoted, then answered with 'قلنا' (we said).
2
Identify the two types of possibility
Sanusi distinguishes between something being possible in itself ('من حيث هو هو') and being absolutely possible ('مطلقاً'). Understand the difference.
The phrase 'ليس كل ما كان عدمه من حيث هو هو جائز' introduces the distinction.
3
Focus on the cause
The reason absolute possibility may be impossible is due to the impossibility of the cause ceasing. Ask: what cause is being referred to?
The cause here is likely the cause of time, which is eternal according to the author's school.
4
Apply to the objection
The objector assumed that if the present now can cease in itself, then absolute cessation is possible. Sanusi shows this is invalid because the cause of time may not cease.
This resolves the objection without denying the possibility of the now ceasing in itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما قوله رابعاً : ( بتقدير أن يفنى الآن الحاضر، ولا يوجد عقيبه آن، فإنه ينقطع الزمان : قلنا : ليس كل ما كان عدمه من حيث هو هو جائز، كان عدمه مطلقاً جائزاً . فربما امتنع عدمه، لا متناع علته
As for his fourth saying: 'If we suppose that the present now ceases to exist, and no moment comes after it, then time would be cut off.' We said: Not everything whose non-existence in itself is possible has its absolute non-existence possible. For perhaps its non-existence is impossible due to the impossibility of its cause's non-existence.
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objector argues that if the present moment (al-an al-hadir) perishes and nothing follows, time would stop, suggesting time is not eternal. The answer distinguishes between possibility per se and absolute possibility: something may be possible in itself but impossible due to external factors, like the impossibility of its cause ceasing. Thus, the cessation of time might be impossible because its cause cannot cease to exist.

Key Terms
الآن الحاضر
العدم
العلة
الجائز
الممتنع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If something is possible in itself, it must be absolutely possible.
Correction: External factors, such as the necessity of a cause, can render absolute impossibility.

Checkpoint
What reason does the author give for why time might not cease even if the present moment perishes?
A) Time is eternal by definition.
B) The non-existence of time may be impossible due to the impossibility of its cause's non-existence.
C) The present moment cannot perish.
D) The objection is invalid.

Key Takeaway
Something may be possible in itself yet impossible absolutely due to external factors, such as the impossibility of its cause ceasing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
