The Objection of Time and Motion Requiring Precedence and Its Deferral
Author defers answer to objection combining time and motion with pre-eternity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the structure of this objection involving time, motion, and pre-eternity, and why the author chooses to defer its answer to a later chapter.

Core Idea
The author defers the answer to an objection about time and motion to build his larger argument.

In this passage, the author presents an objection from an opponent who argues that time and motion inherently require being preceded by something else, which contradicts pre-eternity. Instead of giving an immediate refutation, the author promises to address it later in a dedicated chapter. This deferral is a deliberate pedagogical and rhetorical strategy, not an evasion. Understanding this objection is key to appreciating the overall debate on the eternity of motion.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Identify three parts: the claim about the essence of time and motion, the claim about pre-eternity, and the conclusion that combining is impossible.
2
Examine the author's response
Note that the author does not directly answer but says 'we will answer in the chapter of your proofs...' This is a deferral.
3
Consider why the author defers
The author might be structuring his work to first establish foundational principles before tackling this specific objection. This is a common kalam technique.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قوله ثالثاً : ماهية الزمان والحركة تقتضي المسبوقية بالغير ، والأزلية تنافي المسبوقية بالغير ، والجمع بينهما محال ، قلنا : سنجيب عن هذا الكلام في باب دلائلكم على أن للحركات أزلا وبداية .
And as for his third statement: 'The essence of time and motion necessitates being preceded by another, and pre-eternity is incompatible with being preceded by another, and combining them is impossible.' We said: We will answer this speech in the chapter of your proofs that motions have no beginning and a beginning.
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer pair. The opponent argues that time and motion, by their essence, require being preceded by something else, while pre-eternity (al-azaliyya) negates being preceded. Therefore, it is impossible to combine the two (i.e., to say that time is both pre-eternal and involves motion). The author responds not by directly refuting but by deferring: he promises to answer this in a later chapter dedicated to the proofs that motions have both no beginning and a beginning (أزلا وبداية). The phrase 'أزلا وبداية' appears contradictory but may be interpreted in context. The author's strategy is to show that the objection will be fully addressed later.

Key Terms
ماهية الزمان
الحركة
المسبوقية بالغير
الأزلية
قلنا

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author is avoiding the question.
Correction: The author is structuring his work to address this objection in its appropriate context, a common scholarly practice.

Checkpoint
What does the opponent claim about the essence of time and motion in the objection?
A) They are both eternal.
B) They require being preceded by another.
C) They are both created.
D) They are incompatible with each other.

Key Takeaway
The author defers a complex objection about time and motion to a later chapter, demonstrating a structured approach to argumentation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a difficult objection to your faith, do you have the patience to defer your answer and trust that a proper response will come in time, or do you feel compelled to give an immediate, possibly weak, answer? How does this lesson's example of structured argumentation challenge your rush to defend Allah's names and attributes?

?

Refuting the Argument That Time Would Cease
Author refutes objection by distinguishing per se and absolute possibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between per se possibility and absolute possibility as used to refute objections about the continuity of time.

Core Idea
What is possible in itself may be impossible in reality due to its cause.

The objection claims that if the present moment ceases and no moment follows, time would end. The author refutes this by clarifying that possibility is not absolute: even if something's non-existence is conceivable, it may be impossible because its cause cannot cease. This distinction is central to Islamic theological arguments about time and creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the structure: the opponent's statement followed by the author's response starting with 'qulnā'.
The passage is short but packed with logical nuance.
2
Identify the key distinction
Find where the author distinguishes between two types of possibility: per se possibility (min haythu huwa) and absolute possibility. Understand why the distinction matters.
The phrase 'min haythu huwa huwa' is critical—it means 'in itself'.
3
Reflect on causal necessity
Consider how the impossibility of non-existence can stem from the impossibility of the cause ceasing. Think about what this implies for the nature of time.
This connects to broader debates about whether time is created or eternal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما قوله رابعاً : بتقدير أن يفنى الآن الحاضر ، ولا يوجد عقيبه آن ، فإنه ينقطع الزمان : قلنا : ليس كل ما كان عدمه من حيث هو هو جائز ، كان عدمه مطلقاً جائزاً . فربما امتنع عدمه ، لا متناع علته
And as for his fourth statement: 'Assuming that the present moment perishes and no moment occurs after it, then time would cease.' We said: Not everything whose non-existence is possible in itself is absolutely possible. Perhaps its non-existence is impossible due to the impossibility of its cause.
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage is another objection-and-answer pair. The opponent hypothesizes that if the present 'now' perishes and no subsequent 'now' comes into existence, then time would be interrupted. The author responds by drawing a distinction: the possibility of non-existence can be considered in itself (min haythu huwa huwa) or absolutely. Just because something's non-existence is possible in itself does not mean it is absolutely possible, because its non-existence might be impossible due to the impossibility of its cause. In other words, time may have a cause that necessitates its continuity, so even if we imagine it ceasing, that does not prove it can actually cease. This refutes the opponent's claim that time would necessarily cease under that assumption.

Key Terms
الآن الحاضر
العدم
الجواز
علة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If something's non-existence is conceivable, then it is absolutely possible.
Correction: The author distinguishes: something may be possible in itself but its non-existence is impossible because its cause cannot cease.

Checkpoint

A) Between temporal and eternal.
B) Between per se possibility and absolute possibility.
C) Between essence and accident.
D) Between cause and effect.

Key Takeaway
The key takeaway is that possibility is not one-dimensional; causal necessity can make non-existence impossible even if it is conceivable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
